Salah — Prayer
intermediate
6 min
The Prescribed Dhikr After Salah: Counting and Exceeding
Teaching the dhikr numbers and refuting the idea that exceeding nullifies reward.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the prescribed dhikr after salah and explain whether exceeding the number forfeits the reward, citing the hadith of the poor emigrants.

Core Idea
The number of tasbih after salah is recommended, not a limit – increasing it only increases reward.

You already know that after each obligatory prayer, the Prophet ﷺ recommended saying Subhanallah, Alhamdulillah, and Allahu Akbar 33 times each. But what if you say them more than that? Does the extra count spoil the reward? This lesson examines a key Hanafi clarification: the numbers are recommended, not mandatory limits. The source text, from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi, quotes Badr al-'Ayni's refutation of the idea that exceeding nullifies the reward, and presents the famous hadith of the poor emigrants.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being discussed? It is the dhikr after salah: tasbih, tahmid, takbir, and tahlil. The ruling is a confirmed sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah).
The act is not wajib or fard; it is highly recommended.
2
Locate the hadith proof
Two primary hadiths are cited: Muslim's hadith about forgiveness of sins, and Bukhari's hadith of the poor emigrants. Identify the main proof for the number and the reward.
The hadith from Muslim: 'Whoever says ... after every prayer, his sins are forgiven even if like the foam of the sea.'
3
Understand the contested issue
Some scholars claimed that the reward is tied to the precise number (33 each) and that exceeding it intentionally would forfeit that specific reward. Why might they think that?
They saw it as a 'hadd' (limit) that should not be exceeded.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Badr al-'Ayni refutes this: these numbers are not hudud (legal boundaries) but recommended quantities. Obedience has no limit (الطاعة لا حصر فيها). Increasing is praiseworthy.
The key principle: 'al-ta'a la hasara fiha' – obedience is not confined.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a person who says Subhanallah 40 times instead of 33. According to the Hanafi position, does he lose the reward? No, because the number is not a barrier. He may hope for even more reward.
The practice of using a rosary (subha) or fingers is also discussed; the preference is finger-counting.

مجلس واحد أم لا قلت : كل ذلك ليس بشرط لكن الأفضل أن يأتي به متتابعاً في الوقت الذي عين فيه وصح أنه كان يعقد التسبيح بيمينه وورد أنه قال : واعقدوه بالأنامل فإنهن مسؤولات مستنطقات وجاء بسند ضعيف عن علي Allah thirty-three times, and say Allahu Akbar thirty-three times, that is ninety-nine, and completing the hundred with 'La ilaha illa Allah, alone without partner, His is the Kingdom and His is the praise, and He is over all things competent' - his sins are forgiven even if they were like the foam of the sea. Reported by Muslim. And in what we presented earlier is an indication of its like, and it is the hadith of the emigrants. For the saying of Abu Umama: 'It was said: O Messenger of Allah, which supplication is heard?' He said: 'The middle of the last night and after the prescribed prayers.' Mentioned by Badr al-'Ayni in his commentary on Sahih al-Bukhari and by Mullah 'Ali in his commentary on Mishkat al-Masabih. And in performing thirty-three, there is performing less than that. Badr al-'Ayni said: 'So what is said falls away, that these numbers following prayers of dhikr, if they have a specific number with a specific reward, then the one who performs them exceeding the number intentionally does not attain that reward promised for performing the incomplete number; perhaps those numbers have a wisdom and property that are lost by exceed the numbers and transgressing them. And this is nothing but incoherence. The correct is what we have said because these numbers are not among the boundaries that are forbidden to be exceeded and transgressed; rather they are what competitors compete in and the desirous desire, and obedience has no limit.' If you say: Is the condition for achieving the sunnah and the promised virtue that he says the specified dhikr with the number consecutively or not, and in one sitting or not? I say: All that is not a condition, but it is best to do it consecutively in the time designated for it. And it is authentic that he used to count the tasbih with his right hand, and it is reported that he said: 'Count with the fingertips, for they will be questioned and will speak.' And it came with a weak chain from 'Ali marfu': 'Blessed is the one who remembers with the subha.' Ibn Hajar said: 'The reports of doing tasbih with pebbles and pebbles are many from the Companions and some of the Mothers of the Believers, rather he saw them and approved of them.' And counting tasbih with the fingertips is better than the subha. It is said: If one fears error, the fingers are better, otherwise the subha is better. Thus in Sharh al-Mishkat. His saying: (And in what we presented) he presented it recently with the wording: And his saying, peace be upon him, 'The poor emigrants: you say Subhanallah, Allahu Akbar, and Alhamdulillah after every prayer' etc., does not necessitate... His saying: (And it is the hadith of the emigrants) explanation of what he presented. Al-Bukhari narrated from Abu Hurairah, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: The poor came to the Prophet, peace be upon him, and said: 'The wealthy have gone with the high ranks and everlasting bliss; they pray as we pray, they fast as we fast, and they have surplus wealth with which they perform Hajj, 'Umrah, jihad, and charity.' He said: 'Shall I not tell you something by which you will catch up with those who preceded you, and none after you will catch up with you, and you will be the best of those among whom you live, except for those who do the same? You say Subhanallah, Alhamdulillah, and Allahu Akbar thirty-three times after every prayer.' End.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the specific dhikr (remembrances) after the obligatory prayers. The author, 'Allamah al-Tahtawi, in his Hashiya on Maraqi al-Falah, discusses the well-known practice of saying Subhanallah (33 times), Alhamdulillah (33 times), and Allahu Akbar (33 times), completing the hundred with 'La ilaha illa Allah...' He cites the hadith from Muslim that whoever does this will have their sins forgiven. A key issue addressed here is whether exceeding the prescribed number (e.g., saying each phrase more than 33 times) nullifies the promised reward. Some scholars proposed that because the reward is tied to a specific number, exceeding it would forfeit that reward. Al-Tahtawi, quoting Badr al-'Ayni, refutes this, arguing that these numbers are not legal limits (hudud) that cannot be exceeded; rather, they are recommended acts where increasing is praiseworthy. Obedience has no limit. The condition for attaining the sunnah and its virtue is simply performing the dhikr, not necessarily in one sitting or consecutively, though that is better. The passage also notes the recommended method of counting: using the fingertips of the right hand is superior, but using a rosary (subha) is permissible, especially if one fears error. The hadith of the poor emigrants (from Bukhari) is then quoted in full, illustrating the virtue of this dhikr and how the poor Companions could attain the same reward as the wealthy through these words.

Key Terms
التسبيح (al-tasbih): Saying Subhanallah (glory be to Allah) – a form of dhikr after salah, typically 33 times.
السبحة (al-subha): The rosary or prayer beads used for counting dhikr, permissible but counting with fingertips is preferred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this text, what happens if someone says Subhanallah more than 33 times after salah?
A) The reward for the prescribed dhikr is lost.
B) The extra count is considered bid'ah and sinful.
C) The act is still virtuous and the person may hope for reward, though the specific reward for completing the count may only apply to the prescribed number.
D) The salah itself becomes invalid.

Key Takeaway
The prescribed numbers of tasbih, tahmid, and takbir after salah are recommended quantities, not legal limits; exceeding them is praiseworthy and does not forfeit the promised reward.

Etiquette of Supplication After Salah
Details the etiquettes of du'a after prayer, including starting with oneself, raising hands, and concluding with a Qur'anic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended etiquettes of du'a after salah according to the Hanafi school, identify which acts are disliked or excessive, and apply the proof texts to justify these rulings.

Core Idea
Du'a after salah is a confirmed sunnah; its etiquette balances humility, persistence, and avoidance of excess.

The du'a after the obligatory prayer is an opportunity for intimate communication with Allah. The Hanafi school, drawing from the Qur'an and Sunnah, prescribes a set of etiquettes that ensure the supplication is offered in the most befitting manner. This lesson unpacks those etiquettes, from the order of supplication to the physical posture, clarifying common misconceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the recommended etiquettes
Read through the passage and list the etiquettes mentioned: beginning with oneself, persistence and repetition, avoiding looking up, avoiding excess (i'tida'), lowering the voice, raising hands to shoulders/chest, palms facing sky, not placing hands on ground, and concluding with the verse 'Subhana Rabbika...'.
The passage also mentions the permissibility of praying for all Muslims generally.
2
Classify each as recommended (mandub) or disliked (makruh)
Determine the hukm of each etiquette. For example, raising hands is mustahabb, looking up is makruh, being excessive is makruh. Use the text's explicit statements about 'yukrah' and 'yambaghi'.
Some acts, like repeating three times, are explicitly recommended.
3
Link each etiquette to its proof
For each etiquette, find the hadith or Quranic verse cited. For example, 'Begin with yourself' is from a hadith; 'low voice' is from the adab as mentioned in al-Adhkar; 'raise hands' is from Sharh al-Hisn al-Hasin and hadith.
Some proofs are indirectly referenced; note that the author uses the phrase 'as in Sharh al-Hisn al-Hasin' to indicate the source.
4
Analyze the dispute on al-i'tida' (excess)
The passage lists multiple interpretations of what constitutes excess: asking for impossible, asking for high ranks (like prophethood), shouting, forced rhyming, verbosity, or asking for something unknown. Consider which of these are clearly prohibited (haram) versus disliked (makruh).
The author does not rank these; they are all taken as forms of excess.
5
Apply the rulings to a hypothetical scenario
Consider a scenario: A person after maghrib prays loudly, looks up to the sky, and asks for Paradise without mentioning others. Identify which etiquettes are violated and state whether the du'a is still valid and the recommended action.
Du'a is valid in any form, but missing etiquettes makes it incomplete (naqis) and may lead to makruh.

Then they supplicate for themselves, beginning with themselves because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'Begin with yourself' hadith, and although it was concerning spending, the scholars also use it in matters of the afterlife; they said: a scholar must begin with his family in education, supported by His saying: 'Protect yourselves and your families from a Fire' (Q 66:6), mentioned by al-Abyari in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir. His saying: (with the transmitted comprehensive supplications) and it is recommended to persist in supplication time after time and to repeat it three times, and it is disliked to raise one's gaze to the sky because it lacks etiquette and suggests direction, and the Prophet, peace be upon him, forbade that as in Sharh al-Hisn al-Hasin, and to single out a particular prayer or time for a supplication because it hardens the heart, and to be excessive in supplication because of His saying: 'Indeed He loves the transgressors' (Q 7:55). Its interpretation differs: it is said that he prays for something impossible by law or reason; it is said that he asks for what is not befitting for him, like the ranks of prophets; it is said that it is shouting; it is said that it is forced rhyming; it is said that it is verbosity; it is said that it is asking for something whose reality is not known. And the author benefitted by his saying 'and for the Muslims' the permissibility of supplicating for them generally, because of His saying, narrating from Ibrahim: 'My Lord, forgive me and my parents and the believers on the Day when the account is established' (Q 14:41), and His saying: 'And seek forgiveness for your sin and for the believing men and believing women' (Q 40:40). And it is not necessary that asking forgiveness means that they will be forgiven, for it may not be answered; and in supplication for forgiveness there is showing one's neediness to Allah. And assuming it is answered, it does not necessarily mean that all sins are forgiven, for some sins may be forgiven while others not, as mentioned by Ibn al-'Imad. And by this, what al-Iraqi mentioned regarding the prohibition of supplicating for the forgiveness of all sins for the believers is invalidated. His saying: (And Allah, indeed I love you...) it is recommended to act upon it because it is the advice of the beloved to the beloved. And from the etiquette of supplication is that he supplicates with humility, submissiveness, and low voice, i.e., between whispering and loudness, as in al-Adhkar from al-Ahya', so that it is closer to being answered. His saying: (opposite the chest, and their palms facing the face) which is in al-Hisn al-Hasin, and its explanation: he raises them opposite his shoulders, spreading his palms towards the sky because it is the direction of supplication. End. Some virtuous scholars said: There is no contradiction between them because what is meant is that he should not face their palms to the ground, and the difference in the amount of raising is slight, as indicated in Abu Dawud from Ibn 'Abbas who said: 'The question is that you raise your hands opposite your shoulders or lower.' As for what is narrated that he raised his hands until the whiteness of his armpits appeared, it is interpreted as showing permissibility or in the case of Istisqa' and similar severe calamities and exaggeration in supplication. And in al-Nahr: The recommended manner is that there be a gap between the palms even if slight, and that he should not place one hand on the ground. If he is unable to raise his hands due to an excuse or cold, he may indicate with the index finger, it suffices. End. But in Sharh al-Hisn al-Hasin: And the apparent is that etiquette also includes joining the hands and pointing fingers towards the Qibla. And in Sharh al-Mishkat: It is reported that on the Day of 'Arafah he joined his palms in supplication, and if what is meant by joining in speech is complete closeness, it does not contradict the existence of a small gap. And as for his saying: 'He joined his palms' it does not negate that, because the meaning is he joined them in raising and did not separate one. (Lord of Glory) i.e., might, and it is said that it is a great serpent surrounding the Throne, its tail near its head; when they meet, the Hour will come. His saying: (Whoever desires to fill with the fullest measure) what is meant is multiplying the reward.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the etiquette of supplication (du'a) after the obligatory prayers. The author, commenting on the matn, emphasizes that the supplicant should begin with themselves, citing the hadith 'Begin with yourself' and the principle that a scholar should start with his family. The du'a should be made with persistence, repeating three times, and it is disliked to look up to the sky or to be excessive (al-Itida'), which is interpreted in various ways, such as asking for impossible things, seeking high ranks of prophets, or shouting. It is permissible to pray for all Muslims in general, supported by Quranic verses where Ibrahim and the Prophet are instructed to pray for believers; the author refutes the claim that one cannot pray for forgiveness of all sins, arguing that even if not answered, the du'a expresses need. The hadith about Mu'adh ('O Allah, help me to remember You...') is cited as a recommended du'a after salah. The etiquette of raising hands is detailed: they should be raised to the chest or shoulders, palms facing the sky, with a slight gap, not placed on the ground. In case of inability (e.g., cold), gesturing with a finger suffices. The conclusion of du'a with the verse 'Subhana Rabbika Rabb al-'Izzati 'amma yasifun' (Q 37:180) is recommended, as it multiplies reward.

Key Terms
الدعاء (al-du'a): Supplication to Allah; here specifically the supererogatory du'a after salah, distinct from recitations during salah.
الاعتداء في الدعاء (al-i'tida' fi al-du'a): Excessive or improper behavior in supplication, such as asking for impossible things, shouting, or forced rhyming.
السجع (al-saj'): Rhyming prose; in du'a, forced or artificial rhyming is considered excessive and disliked.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Du'a after salah is a sunnah with specific etiquettes: begin with yourself, be persistent, avoid excess, raise hands appropriately, and conclude with the Quranic verse Subhana Rabbika.

Ruling on Wiping Face After Du'a and Raising Hands
Ruling on wiping face after du'a and raising hands, with proof from hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wiping the face after du'a and identify the condition under which it is recommended.

Core Idea
Wiping face after du'a is mustahab if hands were raised; not required if not.

In this lesson, we examine the practice of wiping the face with the hands after making supplication. The Hanafi school, based on a hadith, considers this a recommended act (mustahab) when the hands have been raised during du'a. However, scholars differ in its status, and we will explore the proof and the conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is wiping the face with both hands after completing a supplication. According to the Hanafi school, this is mustahab (recommended).
Not wajib, as the command is interpreted in context.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith: 'When you supplicate Allah, supplicate with the palms of your hands... and when you finish, wipe your face with them.' (Abu Dawood, Tirmidhi).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The command 'amsah' (wipe) is interpreted as recommendation due to the context of du'a and the lack of strict obligation in the Prophet's practice.
In Hanafi usul, a command implies obligation unless a qarina indicates otherwise.
4
Note the condition
Wiping is linked to raising hands; if hands are not raised (common in many du'as), wiping is not recommended.
The Prophet frequently made du'a without raising hands and did not wipe.
5
Evaluate the ikhtilaf
A minority view considers wiping bid'ah, but the majority (including Hanafis) regard it as mustahab based on authentic hadith.
The disagreement is over authenticity and interpretation of the hadith.

Then they wipe (their faces) with them (i.e., their hands) at the end, because of his saying: 'When you supplicate Allah, supplicate with the palms of your hands, and do not supplicate with their backs. When you finish, wipe your face with them.' And it was his practice that when he raised his hands in supplication he did not lower them, and in a narration: he did not return them except to wipe his face with them. And Allah Almighty is the Grantor of success.
His statement: 'Then they wipe their faces with them': The wisdom in that is the return of blessing upon him and its penetration to his interior, and as an omen of repelling affliction and obtaining giving. And he should not wipe with one hand because that is the action of the arrogant. And the hadith indicates that if he did not raise his hands in supplication, he does not wipe with them. This is a good restriction because he used to supplicate frequently, such as in prayer, circumambulation, and other supplications recorded after prayers, at sleep, after eating, and the like, and he did not raise his hands nor wipe his face with them. This is mentioned in Sharh al-Mishkat, Sharh al-Hisn al-Hasin, and others.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the recommendation of wiping the face with the hands after supplication (du'a) and the etiquette of raising hands during du'a. It begins by citing the virtue of reciting the verse 'Subhana rabbika' (Surah Sad, verse 83) after prayer, based on a hadith. It then instructs that one should wipe the face with the hands after du'a, referencing the hadith: 'When you supplicate Allah, supplicate with the palms of your hands... and when you finish, wipe your face with them.' The passage emphasizes that wiping should be done with both hands, not one, as that resembles the arrogant. It also clarifies that wiping is done only when hands are raised in du'a; if hands are not raised (as was the Prophet's practice in many situations), then wiping is not done. The commentary explains the wisdom behind wiping: seeking blessing and as a hopeful gesture for repelling harm and receiving good. This ruling is based on explicit hadith and the practice of the Prophet, and it is considered mustahab (recommended) in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
مسح الوجه (mash al-wajh): Wiping the face with the hands after completing a supplication, with the intention of seeking blessing.
دبر الصلاة (dubr al-salah): After the completion of the prayer, before or after saying taslim? Usually after the taslim.

Wiping the face after du'a is a sunnah act when hands are raised, based on an authentic hadith, and should be done with both hands.

The Dispute on Silent vs Loud Dhikr: A Hanafi Reconciliation
Dispute on whether silent or loud dhikr is superior, with Hanafi reconciliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on silent vs loud dhikr and apply the principle of circumstantial preference to determine the better form in a given situation.

Core Idea
Silent dhikr is not always better; the preference depends on whether riya or disturbance is feared.

This passage addresses a classic dispute: Is silent dhikr superior to loud dhikr? The author presents both sides, citing hadiths, and then reconciles them by showing that the ruling varies according to circumstances. Understanding this reconciliation is key to applying the sunnah properly and avoiding unnecessary conflict in mosques today.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that the preference between silent and loud dhikr depends on circumstances: silent is better when one fears riya (showing off) or when it might disturb others; otherwise loud is permissible and sometimes better.
This is a position of tafsil (differentiation), not absolute preference for one form.
2
Identify the opposing views
Some scholars hold that silent dhikr is absolutely better, citing hadiths like 'The best dhikr is the hidden one.' Others hold that loud dhikr is absolutely better, citing the Prophet's practice of raising his voice after prayer.
These are two extreme positions that the Hanafi school reconciles.
3
Follow the proofs for each view
For silent: hadith on hidden dhikr and the value of sincerity. For loud: Ibn al-Zubayr's narration of the Prophet saying 'La ilaha illallah' loudly after prayer, and the command to make Quran recitation heard.
Both sets of proofs are authentic; they need to be harmonized.
4
Analyse the Hanafi reconciliation
The author reconciles by noting that the choice varies according to persons and circumstances: when riya or disturbance is present, silent is better; otherwise loud is better. This respects all hadiths without rejecting any.
This is a classic Hanafi method of jam' (reconciliation) rather than tarjih (preference) or naskh (abrogation).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: In a mosque, loud dhikr is generally allowed and even encouraged to avoid preventing Allah's remembrance, but if it disturbs sleepers, those praying, or Quran reciters, silent becomes better.
This ruling is found in al-Bazzaziyya and supported by al-Sha'rani.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فروع اختلف هل الإسرار في الذكر أفضل فقيل : نعم لأحاديث كثيرة تدل عليه منها خير الذكر الخفي، وخير الرزق ما يكفي ولأن الإسرار أبلغ في الإخلاص، وأقرب إلى الإجابة ، وقيل : الجهر أفضل لأحاديث كثيرة منها ما رواه ابن الزبير كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا سلم من صلاته قال بصوته الأعلى : لا إله إلا الله وحده لا شريك له، وتقدم وقد كان يأمر من يقرأ القرآن في المسجد أن : يسمع قراءته وكان ابن عمر يأمر من يقرأ عليه، وعلى أصحابه وهم يستمعون ولأنه أكثر عملاً وأبلغ في التدبر ونفعه متعد لإيقاظ قلوب الغافلين وجمع بين الأحاديث الواردة بأن ذلك يختلف بحسب الأشخاص، والأحوال فمتى خاف الرياء، أو تأذى أحد كان الإسرار أفضل، ومتى فقد ما ذكر كان الجهر أفضل قال في الفتاوى لا يمنع من الجهز بالذكر في المساجد احترازاً عن الدخول تحت قوله تعالى: (ومن أظلم ممن منع مساجد الله أن يذكر فيها اسمه) [البقرة: ٢] كذا في البزازية ونص الشعراني في ذكر الذاكر للمذكور والشاكر للمشكور ما لفظه وأجمع العلماء سلفاً وخلفاً على استحباب ذكر الله تعالى في المساجد، د، وغيرها من غير نكير إلا أن يشوش جهرهم بالذكر على نائم، أو مصل، أو قاری قرآن كما هو مقرر في كتب الفقه
Branches: They differed: Is silent dhikr better? It was said: Yes, because of many hadiths indicating it, including 'The best dhikr is the hidden one, and the best provision is that which suffices' and because silence is more effective in sincerity and closer to acceptance. And it was said: Loud dhikr is better because of many hadiths, including what Ibn al-Zubayr narrated: The Messenger of Allah, when he finished his prayer, would say in a loud voice: 'There is no god but Allah alone, no partner for Him...' and it has preceded that he used to command those reciting Quran in the mosque to make their recitation heard, and Ibn Umar used to command those reciting to him and his companions to listen. Also because it is more of an action and more effective in reflection, and its benefit extends to awakening the hearts of the heedless. And they reconciled the hadiths by stating that it varies according to persons and circumstances: when one fears riya (showing off) or someone is disturbed, then silence is better; when that is absent, loud is better. He said in al-Fatawa: There is no prohibition of loud dhikr in mosques, to avoid falling under His statement: 'And who is more unjust than he who prevents mosques of Allah from mentioning His name therein?' (al-Baqarah: 2). Thus in al-Bazzaziyya. And al-Sha'rani stated in Dhikr al-Dhakir li al-Madhkoor: 'And the scholars, early and late, agreed upon the recommendation of remembering Allah in mosques and elsewhere without objection, unless their loud dhikr disturbs a sleeper, a person praying, or a Quran reciter, as is established in books of fiqh.'
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the preference between silent and loud dhikr, presented as a dispute within the Hanafi school and across madhhabs. It presents both views: silent is preferred because of hadiths like 'The best dhikr is the hidden one' and because it is more sincere; loud is preferred because the Prophet himself and the Companions raised their voices in dhikr after prayer and in other contexts. The reconciliation is that it depends on circumstances: silent is better when one fears hypocrisy or disturbance to others; otherwise loud is better. The passage also quotes a fatwa that loud dhikr in mosques is permissible and even recommended to avoid preventing the name of Allah from being mentioned, as per the Quranic verse. The Hanafi school generally allows loud dhikr in congregation but with conditions not to disturb others. The conclusion is that both are valid, and the choice depends on the situation.

Key Terms
الإسرار (al-israr): Performing dhikr silently, moving the tongue but not audible to others.
الجهر (al-jahr): Performing dhikr audibly, raising the voice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is silent dhikr preferred over loud dhikr?
A) When one wants to wake up the heedless.
B) When one fears showing off (riya) or disturbing someone.
C) Always, because hidden dhikr is the best.
D) Never, loud dhikr is always better.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school teaches that both silent and loud dhikr have their place, and the best choice depends on avoiding riya and not disturbing others.

Rulings on Group Quran Recitation and Recitation in the Bathhouse
Rulings on group recitation and bathhouse recitation with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which group recitation is disliked and recitation in a bathhouse is permissible according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Group recitation is disliked if it prevents listening; recitation in a bathhouse is allowed if private parts are covered and place is pure.

The text from al-Halabi and al-Durra al-Munifa provides nuanced rulings on where and how to recite Quran. Understanding these conditions helps avoid common pitfalls in congregational settings and maintain proper adab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the ruling for group recitation (disliked if it leads to abandoning listening) and recitation in a bathhouse (permissible with conditions).
Group recitation has an alternative view of no harm.
2
List conditions for permissibility
For recitation in a bathhouse: (1) no one exposed, (2) place is pure. Then loud or silent is okay. Otherwise only silent recitation is allowed.
These conditions prevent disrespect.
3
Distinguish between different scenarios
Group recitation is only disliked when it prevents listening; if people listen attentively, it may be permissible. Reciting in a bathhouse varies based on presence of uncovered persons.
Context matters.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some scholars say no harm in group recitation; the relied-upon position in the text is dislike.
The mufta bihi is the dislike.
5
Apply to modern settings
For example, group recitation in a halaqa where everyone reads simultaneously is disliked; recitation in a gym shower should be silent if others are present.
Real-world application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الحلبي الأفضل الجهر بالقراءة إن لم يكن عند قوم مشغولين ما لم يخالطه رياء اهـ، وفي الدرة المنيفة عن القنية يكره للقوم أن يقرؤوا القرآن جملة لتضمنها ترك الاستماع والإنصات، وقيل: لا بأس به، اهـ وفيها أيضاً قراءة القرآن في الحمام إن لم يكن ثمة أحد مكشوف العورة، وكان الموضع طاهراً تجوز جهراً وخفية وإن لم يكن كذلك فإن قرأ في نفسه فلا بأس به، ويكره الجهر اهـ وفي الدر من الكراهة أيضاً الترجيع بالقراءة والأذان بالصوت الطيب طيب ما لم يزد حرفاً فيكره له ولمستمعه، وقول جماعة القائل لمن زاد ذلك حين سكت أحسنت إن لسكوته فحسن وإن لتلك القراءة يخشى عليه الكفر اهـ وفيه أيضاً التغني بالقرآن، إذا لم يخرج بألحانه عن قدر ما هو صحيح في العربية مستحسن، والتغني حرام إذا كان بذكر امرأة معينة حية، أو وصف خمر يهيج إليها، أو قصد هجو ولو لذمي وأجاز بعضهم الغناء في العرس كضرب الدف فيه، ومنهم من أ أباحه مطلقاً، ومنهم من كرهه مطلقاً ذكره العيني وتبعه الباقاني قلت لكن في البحر، والمذهب حرمته مطلقاً فانقطع الاختلاف بل ظاهر الهداية أنها كبيرة ولو لنفسه، وهو قول شيخ الإسلام: وكذا لسامعه وحاضره اهـ من سكب الأنهر ملخصاً وذكر ابن الجزري في الحصن الحصين أن كل ذكر مشروع أي مأمور به في الشرع واجباً كان أو مستحباً لا يعتد بشيء منه حتى يتلفظ به، ويسمع به نفسه اهـ والمعنى أنه إذا قرأ في قلبه حال القراءة أو سبح بقلبه في الركوع والسجود لا يكون آتياً بفرض القراءة، وسنة التسبيح، وإلا فقد أخرج أبو يعلى عن عائشة (1) أفضل الذكر الخفي الذي لا يسمعه الحفظة سبعون ضعفاً الخ، وأما الرقص والتصفيق، والصريخ وضرب الأوتار، والصنج والبوق الذي يفعله بعض من يدعي التصوف فإنه حرام بالإجماع لأنهازي الكفار كما في سكب الأنهر، وفي مجمع الأنهر عن التسهيل الوجد مراتب وبعضه يسلب الاختيار فلا وجه المطلق الإنكار وفي التتارخانية ما يدل على جوازه للمغلوب الذي حركاته كحركات المرتعش اهـ
And in al-Halabi: The best is loud recitation if not in the presence of occupied people, as long as ostentation is not mixed with it. End. And in al-Durra al-Munifa from al-Qunyah: It is disliked for a group to recite the Quran collectively because it entails abandoning listening and paying attention. And it was said: There is no harm in it. End. And in it also: Recitation of the Quran in the bathroom – if there is no one with exposed private parts and the place is pure – is permissible loudly and silently; otherwise, if he recites to himself, there is no harm, but loud recitation is disliked. End. And in al-Durr: Also from disliked things is tarji' (embellishment) in recitation and the call to prayer with a pleasant voice, as long as no letter is added; then it is disliked for him and his listener. And the statement of a group who said to the one who increased that when he remained silent: 'You have done well!' – if his silence is good, but that recitation makes one fear disbelief for him. End. And in it also: Singing (taghanni) with the Quran – if he does not, with its melodies, exceed what is correct in Arabic, it is approved (mustahsan). And singing is haram if it is mentioning a specific living woman, or describing wine that incites towards it, or if it is intended as satire even against a dhmmi. And some allowed singing at a wedding, including beating the duff, and some permitted it absolutely, and some disliked it absolutely. Al-Ayni mentioned it, and al-Baqani followed him. I say: But in al-Bahr, the madhhab is its absolute prohibition, so the difference is settled. Rather, the apparent of al-Hidayah is that it is a major sin even if done for oneself, and that is the statement of Shaykh al-Islam. Similarly for its listener and attendee. End from Sakb al-Anhur summarized. And Ibn al-Jazari mentioned in al-Hisn al-Hasin that every prescribed dhikr, that is, commanded by the Law, whether obligatory or recommended, nothing of it is considered valid until one utters it and makes oneself hear it. End. The meaning is that if one recites in his heart during reading or says tasbih in his heart in bowing and prostration, he is not fulfilling the obligatory recitation or the sunnah of tasbih. Otherwise, Abu Ya'la narrated from Aisha: 'The best dhikr is the hidden one, which the recording angels do not hear, seventy times better...' etc. As for dancing, clapping, screaming, playing strings, cymbals, and trumpets that some pretenders to Sufism do, it is haram by consensus because it is imitation of the disbelievers, as in Sakb al-Anhur. And in Majma' al-Anhur from al-Tashil: ecstatic states have levels, and some of them remove free will, so there is no absolute prohibition. And in al-Tatarkhaniyya, there is indication of permissibility for the one overcome, whose movements are like those of a trembling person. End.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage covers multiple rulings on Quran recitation and related practices. First, it discusses group recitation: it is disliked if it leads to ignoring listening and attention, though some say no harm. Second, recitation in a bathroom: permitted if private parts are covered and place is pure; otherwise quiet recitation is fine but loud is disliked. Third, tarji' (embellishment) in Quran and adhan: disliked if letters are not added; if letters are added, it becomes severely problematic (fear of disbelief for the reciter). Fourth, taghanni (singing/melodic recitation) of Quran: if it stays within Arabic correctness, it is acceptable; if it becomes like singing about women or wine, it is haram. The passage then discusses singing (ghina) generally: some allowed it in weddings, but the Hanafi madhhab according to al-Bahr and al-Hidayah declares it absolutely haram, a major sin. Then a crucial point: dhikr and recitation must be pronounced audibly enough for oneself to hear; internal recitation without moving the tongue does not fulfill the obligation or sunnah. Finally, dancing, clapping, and musical instruments used by some Sufis are haram by consensus as imitation of disbelievers, though ecstatic movements beyond control may be excused.

Key Terms
الجماعة (al-jama'ah): Group of people reciting together.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on reciting the Quran aloud in a bathhouse when someone is uncovered?
A) Permissible always.
B) Disliked.
C) Forbidden.
D) Recommended.

Key Takeaway
Group recitation is disliked if it hinders listening; recitation in a bathhouse is permissible when privacy and purity are maintained.

Rulings on Melodic Recitation (Tarji' and Taghanni) and Singing (Ghina)
Rulings on tarji', taghanni, and ghina with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between permissible and impermissible melodic recitation and state the Hanafi ruling on singing (ghina).

Core Idea
Melodic recitation is allowed if Arabic rules are preserved; singing is absolutely haram in the Hanafi school.

This passage addresses the fine line between beautifying Quranic recitation and crossing into prohibited musical performance. Understanding these distinctions protects one's faith and practice.

Guided Path
1
Define tarji' and taghanni
Tarji' is embellishing the voice, possibly adding letters; taghanni is singing/melodic recitation. Identify each term in the text.
Tarji' is more about vocal embellishment, taghanni about melody.
2
Identify the conditions for tarji'
Tarji' is disliked if no letter is added; if a letter is added, it becomes severely problematic (fear of kufr for praising it).
The listener also incurs dislike.
3
Identify the conditions for taghanni
Taghanni is mustahsan (approved) if it stays within correct Arabic; if it becomes like singing about women or wine, it becomes haram.
The ruling depends on content and effect.
4
Analyze the ruling on general singing (ghina)
Some scholars allowed singing at weddings; but the Hanafi madhhab in al-Bahr and al-Hidayah declares it absolutely haram, a major sin.
This is the relied-upon position.
5
Synthesize the differences
Compare the allowances: Quranic taghanni is different from secular singing. The key is maintaining Arabic correctness and avoiding forbidden content.
The mufta bihi for ghina is haram.

The Requirement of Audible Recitation and the Prohibition of Imitative Practices
Requirement of audible pronunciation for dhikr and prohibition of musical/dance practices

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the requirement that dhikr must be audibly pronounced to oneself, and identify the prohibition of dancing and musical instruments as imitation of disbelievers.

Core Idea
Dhikr and Quran recitation must be pronounced audibly enough to hear oneself; dancing and music are haram as imitation of disbelievers.

This passage clarifies a critical condition for the validity of dhikr: it must be uttered with the tongue, not just thought. It also addresses practices associated with some Sufi groups, ruling them haram by consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Ibn al-Jazari states that every prescribed dhikr (obligatory or recommended) is not valid unless pronounced audibly enough for oneself to hear.
This applies to Quran recitation in prayer and tasbih in ruku/sajdah.
2
Distinguish between internal and audible recitation
Reciting in the heart (without moving tongue) does not fulfill the obligation or sunnah. The exception is the reward for hidden dhikr (hadith of Aisha) which refers to a higher degree, not validity.
The hidden dhikr mentioned in the hadith is a virtue, not a substitution for the audible requirement.
3
Apply the principle to specific acts
In prayer, reciting Fatiha silently in heart does not count; tasbih in ruku must be heard by oneself.
This affects the validity of prayer if one does not move the tongue.
4
Identify the prohibition of dancing and music
Dancing, clapping, screaming, and playing musical instruments (strings, cymbals, trumpets) done by some Sufi claimants are haram by consensus because they imitate disbelievers.
There is an exception for the person overcome by ecstasy (majdhub) who loses control.
5
Note the exceptions
Ecstatic states (wajd) that overpower the person may excuse involuntary movements, similar to a trembling person. This is not license for voluntary practice.
The ruling is based on intention and control.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الحلبي الأفضل الجهر بالقراءة إن لم يكن عند قوم مشغولين ما لم يخالطه رياء اهـ، وفي الدرة المنيفة عن القنية يكره للقوم أن يقرؤوا القرآن جملة لتضمنها ترك الاستماع والإنصات، وقيل: لا بأس به، اهـ وفيها أيضاً قراءة القرآن في الحمام إن لم يكن ثمة أحد مكشوف العورة، وكان الموضع طاهراً تجوز جهراً وخفية وإن لم يكن كذلك فإن قرأ في نفسه فلا بأس به، ويكره الجهر اهـ وفي الدر من الكراهة أيضاً الترجيع بالقراءة والأذان بالصوت الطيب طيب ما لم يزد حرفاً فيكره له ولمستمعه، وقول جماعة القائل لمن زاد ذلك حين سكت أحسنت إن لسكوته فحسن وإن لتلك القراءة يخشى عليه الكفر اهـ وفيه أيضاً التغني بالقرآن، إذا لم يخرج بألحانه عن قدر ما هو صحيح في العربية مستحسن، والتغني حرام إذا كان بذكر امرأة معينة حية، أو وصف خمر يهيج إليها، أو قصد هجو ولو لذمي وأجاز بعضهم الغناء في العرس كضرب الدف فيه، ومنهم من أ أباحه مطلقاً، ومنهم من كرهه مطلقاً ذكره العيني وتبعه الباقاني قلت لكن في البحر، والمذهب حرمته مطلقاً فانقطع الاختلاف بل ظاهر الهداية أنها كبيرة ولو لنفسه، وهو قول شيخ الإسلام: وكذا لسامعه وحاضره اهـ من سكب الأنهر ملخصاً وذكر ابن الجزري في الحصن الحصين أن كل ذكر مشروع أي مأمور به في الشرع واجباً كان أو مستحباً لا يعتد بشيء منه حتى يتلفظ به، ويسمع به نفسه اهـ والمعنى أنه إذا قرأ في قلبه حال القراءة أو سبح بقلبه في الركوع والسجود لا يكون آتياً بفرض القراءة، وسنة التسبيح، وإلا فقد أخرج أبو يعلى عن عائشة (1) أفضل الذكر الخفي الذي لا يسمعه الحفظة سبعون ضعفاً الخ، وأما الرقص والتصفيق، والصريخ وضرب الأوتار، والصنج والبوق الذي يفعله بعض من يدعي التصوف فإنه حرام بالإجماع لأنهازي الكفار كما في سكب الأنهر، وفي مجمع الأنهر عن التسهيل الوجد مراتب وبعضه يسلب الاختيار فلا وجه المطلق الإنكار وفي التتارخانية ما يدل على جوازه للمغلوب الذي حركاته كحركات المرتعش اهـ
And in al-Halabi: The best is loud recitation if not in the presence of occupied people, as long as ostentation is not mixed with it. End. And in al-Durra al-Munifa from al-Qunyah: It is disliked for a group to recite the Quran collectively because it entails abandoning listening and paying attention. And it was said: There is no harm in it. End. And in it also: Recitation of the Quran in the bathroom – if there is no one with exposed private parts and the place is pure – is permissible loudly and silently; otherwise, if he recites to himself, there is no harm, but loud recitation is disliked. End. And in al-Durr: Also from disliked things is tarji' (embellishment) in recitation and the call to prayer with a pleasant voice, as long as no letter is added; then it is disliked for him and his listener. And the statement of a group who said to the one who increased that when he remained silent: 'You have done well!' – if his silence is good, but that recitation makes one fear disbelief for him. End. And in it also: Singing (taghanni) with the Quran – if he does not, with its melodies, exceed what is correct in Arabic, it is approved (mustahsan). And singing is haram if it is mentioning a specific living woman, or describing wine that incites towards it, or if it is intended as satire even against a dhmmi. And some allowed singing at a wedding, including beating the duff, and some permitted it absolutely, and some disliked it absolutely. Al-Ayni mentioned it, and al-Baqani followed him. I say: But in al-Bahr, the madhhab is its absolute prohibition, so the difference is settled. Rather, the apparent of al-Hidayah is that it is a major sin even if done for oneself, and that is the statement of Shaykh al-Islam. Similarly for its listener and attendee. End from Sakb al-Anhur summarized. And Ibn al-Jazari mentioned in al-Hisn al-Hasin that every prescribed dhikr, that is, commanded by the Law, whether obligatory or recommended, nothing of it is considered valid until one utters it and makes oneself hear it. End. The meaning is that if one recites in his heart during reading or says tasbih in his heart in bowing and prostration, he is not fulfilling the obligatory recitation or the sunnah of tasbih. Otherwise, Abu Ya'la narrated from Aisha: 'The best dhikr is the hidden one, which the recording angels do not hear, seventy times better...' etc. As for dancing, clapping, screaming, playing strings, cymbals, and trumpets that some pretenders to Sufism do, it is haram by consensus because it is imitation of the disbelievers, as in Sakb al-Anhur. And in Majma' al-Anhur from al-Tashil: ecstatic states have levels, and some of them remove free will, so there is no absolute prohibition. And in al-Tatarkhaniyya, there is indication of permissibility for the one overcome, whose movements are like those of a trembling person. End.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage covers multiple rulings on Quran recitation and related practices. First, it discusses group recitation: it is disliked if it leads to ignoring listening and attention, though some say no harm. Second, recitation in a bathroom: permitted if private parts are covered and place is pure; otherwise quiet recitation is fine but loud is disliked. Third, tarji' (embellishment) in Quran and adhan: disliked if letters are not added; if letters are added, it becomes severely problematic (fear of disbelief for the reciter). Fourth, taghanni (singing/melodic recitation) of Quran: if it stays within Arabic correctness, it is acceptable; if it becomes like singing about women or wine, it is haram. The passage then discusses singing (ghina) generally: some allowed it in weddings, but the Hanafi madhhab according to al-Bahr and al-Hidayah declares it absolutely haram, a major sin. Then a crucial point: dhikr and recitation must be pronounced audibly enough for oneself to hear; internal recitation without moving the tongue does not fulfill the obligation or sunnah. Finally, dancing, clapping, and musical instruments used by some Sufis are haram by consensus as imitation of disbelievers, though ecstatic movements beyond control may be excused.

Key Terms
الوجد (al-wajd): Ecstatic state that may overcome a person, removing control.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consensus on dancing and playing musical instruments as done by some Sufi claimants?
A) Permissible as spiritual expression.
B) Disliked but allowed.
C) Haram as imitation of disbelievers.
D) Recommended for increasing devotion.

Key Takeaway
Dhikr must be audibly pronounced to be valid; voluntary dancing and music are haram, but involuntary ecstatic movements are excused.

Rulings on Handshaking and Kissing in Islam
Rulings on handshaking and kissing with conditions and ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rulings on handshaking and kissing, including their conditions and the intra-madhhab differences.

Core Idea
Handshaking is sunnah; kissing is permissible when done out of reverence, but disliked if done with lust or resembling non-Muslims.

This lesson covers two common social practices: handshaking and kissing. The Hanafi school provides detailed rulings on their permissibility, method, and exceptions, based on hadith and the opinions of the founding imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on handshaking
Find the explicit statement that handshaking (musafaha) is sunnah in all times. Note the hadith evidence.
The hadith from Abu Dharr establishes the practice.
2
Describe the sunnah method
List the specific elements: pressing palm to palm, facing face to face, with both hands, without a barrier, and taking the thumb.
Taking only the fingers is not considered musafaha.
3
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on kissing
Note the disagreement: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad dislike kissing men, while Abu Yusuf allows it. Later scholars permit it under conditions.
The mufta bihi position is permissibility for scholars and just rulers.
4
Analyze the condition for permissible kissing
Understand that the intention (reverence vs. lust) changes the ruling. If done out of lust, it is only allowed between spouses and master-slave.
This condition is derived from the hadiths collected by Badr al-Ayni.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The later Hanafi authorities (e.g., Badr al-Ayni) and texts like Raf' al-Awa'iq permit kissing the hand of a scholar or just ruler as a sign of respect.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمصافحة سنة في سائر الأوقات لما أخرج أبو داود عن أبي ذر : ما لقيت النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إلا وصافحني الحديث، وفيه اعتنقه مرة وفي القهستاني وغيره هي إلصاق الكف بالكف، وإقبال الوجه بالوجه، فأخذ الأصابع ليس بمصافحة خلافاً للروافض والسنة أن تكون بكلتا يديه وبغير حائل من نحو ثوب وعند اللقاء بعد السلام، وأن يأخذ الإيهام فإن فيه عرقا تتشعب منه المحبة، وفي الهداية ويكره أن يقبل الرجل فم الرجل أو يده، أو شيئاً منه، أو يعانقه في إزار واحد، وقال أبو يوسف : لا بأس بذلك كله اهـ وفي غاية البيان عن الواقعات تقبيل يد العالم أو السلطان العادل جائز وورد في أحاديث ذكرها البدر العيني ما يفيد أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يقبل يده ورجله وكان يقبل الحسن وفاطمة، وقبل صلى الله عليه وسلم عثمان بن مظعون بعد موته، وكذلك قبل الصديق رضي الله تعالى عنه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بعد موته، وقبل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ابن عمه جعفراً بين عينيه، ثم قال البدر العيني: فعلم من مجموع ما ذكرنا إباحة تقبيل اليد والرجل، والكشح والرأس والجبهة والشفتين وبين العينين، ولكن كل ذلك إذا كان على وجه المبرة، والإكرام، وأما إذا كان ذلك على وجه الشهوة فلا يجوز إلا في حق الزوجين اهـ أي والسيد وأمته وفي رفع العوائق عن البحر الزاخر لا بأس بتقبيل يد العالم، والسلطان
And handshaking (musafaha) is sunnah in all times, because Abu Dawood narrated from Abu Dharr: 'I never met the Prophet except that he shook hands with me.' And in it he embraced him once. And in al-Qahistani and others: It is pressing palm to palm and facing face to face. Taking the fingers is not handshaking, contrary to the Rawafid. And the sunnah is to do it with both hands and without a barrier like a cloth, and at meeting after the greeting, and to take the thumb, for there is a vein in it from which love branches. And in al-Hidayah: It is disliked for a man to kiss the mouth of another man, or his hand, or any part of him, or to embrace him in a single garment. And Abu Yusuf said: There is no harm in all of that. End. And in Ghayat al-Bayan from al-Waqi'at: Kissing the hand of a scholar or a just ruler is permissible. And there are hadiths mentioned by Badr al-Ayni indicating that the Prophet kissed his hand and foot, and he kissed al-Hasan and Fatima, and he kissed Uthman ibn Maz'un after his death, and similarly Abu Bakr kissed the Messenger of Allah after his death, and the Prophet kissed his cousin Ja'far between his eyes. Then Badr al-Ayni said: So from all we have mentioned, it is known that kissing the hand, foot, side, head, forehead, lips, and between the eyes is permissible. But all that is when done out of reverence and honor. As for if it is done out of lust, it is not permissible except between spouses. End. That is, and between a master and his slave-woman. And in Raf' al-Awa'iq from al-Bahr al-Zakhir: There is no harm in kissing the hand of a scholar and the sultan.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage presents rulings on handshaking and kissing. It begins by stating that musafaha (handshaking) is sunnah at all times, based on the hadith of Abu Dharr. The proper method is pressing palm to palm, facing each other, with both hands, without a barrier, and preferably taking the thumb. Taking only fingers is not considered musafaha, contrary to the Rawafid (a Shi'a group). Then it discusses kissing: According to al-Hidayah, it is disliked for a man to kiss another man's mouth, hand, or any part, or to embrace in one garment. However, Abu Yusuf did not see harm. For kissing the hand of a scholar or just ruler, it is permissible according to Ghayat al-Bayan. Badr al-Ayni collected hadiths showing the Prophet kissed his relatives, his daughter, his foot/hand, etc., leading to the conclusion that kissing various parts is permissible as a sign of honor and reverence. However, if done with sexual desire, it is only permissible between spouses and between a master and his concubine. The Hanafi school generally allows kissing the hand of scholars and rulers as a sign of respect, but discourages it if it resembles a practice of non-Muslims or leads to fitna.

Key Terms
المصافحة (al-musafaha): Shaking hands by pressing palms together, facing each other, done with both hands.
التقبيل (al-taqbil): Kissing as a gesture of respect or affection. Ruling depends on intention and relationship.
الروافض (al-rawafid): A derogatory term used by Sunnis for some Shi'a groups who reject the first three caliphs. Here, they are refuted for a different handshake method.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for permissible kissing?
A) The intention must be reverence and honor.
B) It must be done with both hands.
C) It must be free from lust.
D) The person kissed should be deserving of respect like a scholar.

Key Takeaway
Handshaking is a sunnah with specific etiquettes; kissing is permissible when done respectfully, and the mufta bihi allows kissing the hands of scholars and just rulers.

Refining the Ruling on Praising an Addition in Silent Prayer
Refinement of a prior ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on praising someone who adds a recitation while remaining silent during prayer, and explain why such recitation may lead to kufr.

Core Idea
Praising silence is acceptable, but if the silent period included recitation, the praise is dangerous.

This lesson analyzes a subtle point: when a person remains silent in prayer and adds something (possibly recitation), praising him for his silence is fine, but if that addition was actually recitation, it could be tantamount to disbelief. The text refines a previous opinion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that someone (القائل) praised a person who added something (زاد ذلك) while being silent (حين سكت).
The addition is unspecified, but the context suggests it could be extra recitation.
2
Distinguish the object of praise
The praise 'أحسنت' applies only to the silence, not to what was added. The author refines that if the addition was recitation, the praise is misplaced.
This is a critical refinement of a prior statement.
3
Identify the danger
If the addition was Quranic recitation, the transmitter fears kufr (disbelief) for the praiser because he endorses an innovation or disrespect.
The word 'يخشى عليه الكفر' indicates a severe warning.
4
Derive the general principle
The lesson is that one must be careful to praise only correct actions in prayer, not innovations or additions that contradict proper conduct.
This aligns with the Hanafi strictness regarding prayer rites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائل لمن زاد ذلك حين سكت أحسنت إن لسكوته فحسن وإن لتلك القراءة يخشى عليه الكفر اهـ وفيه أيضاً التغني بالقرآن، إذا لم يخرج بألحانه عن قدر ما هو صحيح في العربية مستحسن، والتغني حرام إذا كان بذكر امرأة معينة حية، أو وصف خمر يهيج إليها، أو قصد هجو ولو لذمي وأجاز بعضهم الغناء في العرس كضرب الدف فيه، ومنهم من أ أباحه مطلقاً، ومنهم من كرهه مطلقاً ذكره العيني وتبعه الباقاني قلت لكن في البحر، والمذهب حرمته مطلقاً فانقطع الاختلاف بل ظاهر الهداية أنها كبيرة ولو لنفسه، وهو قول شيخ الإسلام: وكذا لسامعه وحاضره اهـ من سكب الأنهر ملخصاً وذكر ابن الجزري في الحصن الحصين أن كل ذكر مشروع أي مأمور به في الشرع واجباً كان أو مستحباً لا يعتد بشيء منه حتى يتلفظ به، ويسمع به نفسه اهـ والمعنى أنه إذا قرأ في قلبه حال القراءة أو سبح بقلبه في الركوع والسجود لا يكون آتياً بفرض القراءة، وسنة التسبيح، وإلا فقد أخرج أبو يعلى عن عائشة (1) أفضل الذكر الخفي الذي لا يسمعه الحفظة سبعون ضعفاً الخ
He who said to the one who added that while silent: 'You did well,' for his silence is good, but for that recitation, disbelief is feared for him. And in it also: singing the Quran, if it does not deviate in its melodies from what is correct in Arabic, is commendable. And singing is forbidden if it mentions a specific living woman, or describes wine that excites towards it, or intends satire even of a dhimmi. Some permitted singing at weddings like beating the duff, and some permitted it absolutely, and some disliked it absolutely—mentioned by al-Ayni and followed by al-Baqani. I say: But in al-Bahr, the madhhab is its absolute prohibition, so the disagreement is cut off. Rather, the apparent meaning of al-Hidayah is that it is a major sin even for oneself, and it is the saying of Shaykh al-Islam: and likewise for the listener and attendee. End summary from Sakk al-Anhur. And Ibn al-Jazari mentioned in al-Husn al-Hasin that every prescribed remembrance—i.e., commanded in the Shariah, whether obligatory or recommended—is not considered at all until one utters it and hears it himself. The meaning is that if one recites in his heart during recitation, or glorifies in his heart in bowing and prostration, he does not fulfill the obligation of recitation or the sunnah of tasbih. Otherwise, Abu Ya'la narrated from Aisha: 'The best remembrance is the hidden one that the recording angels do not hear, seventy times better.' etc.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
Lesson Type: Ruling statement with compilation of opinions. The text discusses three issues: (1) The case of someone who added something to the prayer while silent; the commentator clarifies that praising his silence is fine but if the addition was recitation, it may lead to disbelief. (2) The ruling on al-taghanni bil-Quran (vocal modulation in recitation): if it remains within correct Arabic prosody it is commendable; if it distorts, it is forbidden. (3) The ruling on singing: it is absolutely forbidden in the Hanafi school, though some earlier scholars permitted it at weddings. The text emphasizes that the relied-upon position is prohibition, and it is considered a major sin. (4) The requirement of vocal utterance for obligatory and supererogatory dhikr: silent, heart-based dhikr does not fulfill the obligation of recitation or the sunnah of tasbih, though extra merit is mentioned for hidden dhikr. The author cites Ibn al-Jazari to stress that verbalization is necessary for validity.

Key Terms
زاد ذلك (zada dhalik): Added that (something extra during the prayer)

Key Takeaway

The Hanafi Ruling on Taghanni bil-Quran and Ghina (Singing)
Compilation of rulings on vocal expression

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between permissible modulation (taghanni) of Quran recitation and absolutely forbidden singing (ghina), and apply the conditions to both.

Core Idea
Taghanni within Arabic prosody is commendable; singing (ghina) with any forbidden content is a major sin.

This lesson covers two types of vocal expression: beautifying Quran recitation (taghanni) and ordinary singing (ghina). The Hanafi school draws a sharp line: taghanni is permissible if it stays within correct Arabic, while ghina is absolutely forbidden, even if alone, and is considered a major sin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The text first discusses taghanni (singing the Quran) and then ghina (secular singing). Recognize that they are distinct in ruling.
Taghanni is a form of vocal modulation, not secular singing.
2
Determine the condition for taghanni
For taghanni, if the melody does not deviate from correct Arabic prosody (مستحسن), it is commendable. If it distorts the words, it becomes forbidden.
The condition is about preserving correct Arabic articulation.
3
Identify the ruling on ghina
The text lists forbidden content (mention of a woman, wine, satire) but then states the school's absolute prohibition, even for oneself, and that it is a major sin (كبيرة).
Earlier disagreements are overridden by the final position.
4
Note the resolution of ikhtilaf
The author mentions that some permitted ghina at weddings or absolutely, but the later relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is absolute prohibition.
The text says 'والمذهب حرمته مطلقاً فانقطع الاختلاف'.
5
Apply to listener and attendee
The prohibition applies to the singer and also to the listener and anyone present, indicating collective responsibility.
The phrase 'وكذا لسامعه وحاضره' extends the sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائل لمن زاد ذلك حين سكت أحسنت إن لسكوته فحسن وإن لتلك القراءة يخشى عليه الكفر اهـ وفيه أيضاً التغني بالقرآن، إذا لم يخرج بألحانه عن قدر ما هو صحيح في العربية مستحسن، والتغني حرام إذا كان بذكر امرأة معينة حية، أو وصف خمر يهيج إليها، أو قصد هجو ولو لذمي وأجاز بعضهم الغناء في العرس كضرب الدف فيه، ومنهم من أ أباحه مطلقاً، ومنهم من كرهه مطلقاً ذكره العيني وتبعه الباقاني قلت لكن في البحر، والمذهب حرمته مطلقاً فانقطع الاختلاف بل ظاهر الهداية أنها كبيرة ولو لنفسه، وهو قول شيخ الإسلام: وكذا لسامعه وحاضره اهـ من سكب الأنهر ملخصاً وذكر ابن الجزري في الحصن الحصين أن كل ذكر مشروع أي مأمور به في الشرع واجباً كان أو مستحباً لا يعتد بشيء منه حتى يتلفظ به، ويسمع به نفسه اهـ والمعنى أنه إذا قرأ في قلبه حال القراءة أو سبح بقلبه في الركوع والسجود لا يكون آتياً بفرض القراءة، وسنة التسبيح، وإلا فقد أخرج أبو يعلى عن عائشة (1) أفضل الذكر الخفي الذي لا يسمعه الحفظة سبعون ضعفاً الخ
He who said to the one who added that while silent: 'You did well,' for his silence is good, but for that recitation, disbelief is feared for him. And in it also: singing the Quran, if it does not deviate in its melodies from what is correct in Arabic, is commendable. And singing is forbidden if it mentions a specific living woman, or describes wine that excites towards it, or intends satire even of a dhimmi. Some permitted singing at weddings like beating the duff, and some permitted it absolutely, and some disliked it absolutely—mentioned by al-Ayni and followed by al-Baqani. I say: But in al-Bahr, the madhhab is its absolute prohibition, so the disagreement is cut off. Rather, the apparent meaning of al-Hidayah is that it is a major sin even for oneself, and it is the saying of Shaykh al-Islam: and likewise for the listener and attendee. End summary from Sakk al-Anhur. And Ibn al-Jazari mentioned in al-Husn al-Hasin that every prescribed remembrance—i.e., commanded in the Shariah, whether obligatory or recommended—is not considered at all until one utters it and hears it himself. The meaning is that if one recites in his heart during recitation, or glorifies in his heart in bowing and prostration, he does not fulfill the obligation of recitation or the sunnah of tasbih. Otherwise, Abu Ya'la narrated from Aisha: 'The best remembrance is the hidden one that the recording angels do not hear, seventy times better.' etc.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
Lesson Type: Ruling statement with compilation of opinions. The text discusses three issues: (1) The case of someone who added something to the prayer while silent; the commentator clarifies that praising his silence is fine but if the addition was recitation, it may lead to disbelief. (2) The ruling on al-taghanni bil-Quran (vocal modulation in recitation): if it remains within correct Arabic prosody it is commendable; if it distorts, it is forbidden. (3) The ruling on singing: it is absolutely forbidden in the Hanafi school, though some earlier scholars permitted it at weddings. The text emphasizes that the relied-upon position is prohibition, and it is considered a major sin. (4) The requirement of vocal utterance for obligatory and supererogatory dhikr: silent, heart-based dhikr does not fulfill the obligation of recitation or the sunnah of tasbih, though extra merit is mentioned for hidden dhikr. The author cites Ibn al-Jazari to stress that verbalization is necessary for validity.

Key Terms
التغني بالقرآن (al-taghanni bil-Quran): Vocal modulation or beautification of voice in Quran recitation within correct Arabic prosody; deemed commendable (mustahsan) if it does not distort the text.
الغناء (al-ghina): Singing that may include forbidden content (mention of a woman, wine, satire); absolutely prohibited in the Hanafi school.
الدف (al-duff): A frame drum used in weddings; some earlier scholars allowed it with singing, but the mufta bihi prohibits the singing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on ghina (singing) in the Hanafi school?
A) Permissible absolutely
B) Permissible only at weddings
C) Disliked
D) Absolutely forbidden, a major sin

Key Takeaway
Taghanni is permissible if it preserves Arabic; ghina is absolutely forbidden in the Hanafi school, and it is a major sin.

The Requirement of Vocal Utterance for Prescribed Dhikr
Clarification of requirement for vocal dhikr vs merit of hidden dhikr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which acts of dhikr require vocal utterance for validity, and differentiate between the merit of hidden dhikr and the obligation of vocal dhikr.

Core Idea
Prescribed dhikr (Quran recitation, tasbih) is not fulfilled by heart alone; vocal utterance is necessary, even though hidden dhikr has greater merit.

This lesson addresses a common confusion: the virtue of silent, hidden dhikr does not replace the requirement to vocalize obligatory or sunnah dhikr. The text from Ibn al-Jazari clarifies that prescribed dhikr must be uttered and heard by oneself, while heart-based remembrance is only for extra reward.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Ibn al-Jazari rules that every prescribed dhikr (واجباً أو مستحباً) is not considered until it is uttered and heard by oneself.
This applies to Quran recitation in prayer, tasbih, etc.
2
Apply to specific acts
If one recites Quran in his heart during prayer (e.g., while the imam is reciting?), or says tasbih in his heart during ruku/sujood, he does not fulfill the obligation (فرض) or sunnah (سنة).
The text explicitly states that heart-recitation does not suffice.
3
Reconcile with virtue of hidden dhikr
The hadith from Aisha states that hidden dhikr is seventy times better. This seems to contradict the rule, but the resolution: hidden dhikr is for supererogatory dhikr, not for prescribed (obligatory or sunnah) dhikr.
The phrase 'وإلا' (otherwise) introduces the hadith to show that hidden dhikr is only for extra reward, not for fulfilling obligations.
4
Derive the practical takeaway
For obligatory and sunnah dhikr, one must move the lips and produce sound audible to oneself. For optional dhikr, silent heart dhikr is highly meritorious.
This prevents the mistake of thinking silent dhikr fulfills prayer requirements.

Source Text

القائل لمن زاد ذلك حين سكت أحسنت إن لسكوته فحسن وإن لتلك القراءة يخشى عليه الكفر اهـ وفيه أيضاً التغني بالقرآن، إذا لم يخرج بألحانه عن قدر ما هو صحيح في العربية مستحسن، والتغني حرام إذا كان بذكر امرأة معينة حية، أو وصف خمر يهيج إليها، أو قصد هجو ولو لذمي وأجاز بعضهم الغناء في العرس كضرب الدف فيه، ومنهم من أ أباحه مطلقاً، ومنهم من كرهه مطلقاً ذكره العيني وتبعه الباقاني قلت لكن في البحر، والمذهب حرمته مطلقاً فانقطع الاختلاف بل ظاهر الهداية أنها كبيرة ولو لنفسه، وهو قول شيخ الإسلام: وكذا لسامعه وحاضره اهـ من سكب الأنهر ملخصاً وذكر ابن الجزري في الحصن الحصين أن كل ذكر مشروع أي مأمور به في الشرع واجباً كان أو مستحباً لا يعتد بشيء منه حتى يتلفظ به، ويسمع به نفسه اهـ والمعنى أنه إذا قرأ في قلبه حال القراءة أو سبح بقلبه في الركوع والسجود لا يكون آتياً بفرض القراءة، وسنة التسبيح، وإلا فقد أخرج أبو يعلى عن عائشة (1) أفضل الذكر الخفي الذي لا يسمعه الحفظة سبعون ضعفاً الخ
He who said to the one who added that while silent: 'You did well,' for his silence is good, but for that recitation, disbelief is feared for him. Some permitted singing at weddings like beating the duff, and some permitted it absolutely, and some disliked it absolutely—mentioned by al-Ayni and followed by al-Baqani. Rulings on Dancing, Clapping, and Handshake
Rulings on dancing, musical instruments, and handshake

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on dancing and musical instruments according to the Hanafi school, explain the exception for overwhelming ecstatic states (wajd), and describe the sunnah method of handshake (musafahah).

Core Idea
Dancing and musical instruments are haram as imitation of disbelievers; overwhelming ecstasy may excuse. Handshake is sunnah with both hands, clasping thumbs.

This lesson examines two distinct rulings from classical Hanafi fiqh. First, the prohibition of dancing, clapping, and musical instruments—often mistakenly considered permissible by some—and the critical exception for involuntary movements during ecstatic states. Second, the confirmed sunnah of handshake (musafahah) with its proper method, distinguishing it from the insufficient practice of merely grasping fingers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited actions and ruling
List the actions mentioned: dancing, clapping, screaming, string instruments, cymbals, trumpet. The text declares them haram by consensus (al-ijma').
The ruling is based on the principle of imitating disbelievers (tashabbuh bi al-kuffār).
2
Understand the exception for wajd
Ecstatic states (wajd) have levels; some remove choice. The text cites al-Tatarkhaniyyah: if a person is so overwhelmed that movements are like tremors, it may be permissible for them.
This is a critical refinement: not all ecstatic movements are sinful; the key is loss of volition.
3
Describe the ruling on handshake
The text states musafahah is sunnah at all times, citing the hadith of Abu Dharr. The sunnah applies in every meeting after the greeting.
The proof is a hadith from Sunan Abi Dawud.
4
List the sunnah conditions for handshake
From the text: (1) press palm to palm, (2) face to face, (3) use both hands, (4) no barrier (e.g., cloth), (5) clasp the thumb of the other person.
Merely taking the fingers is not considered musafahah, contrary to the Rafidah (Shia).
5
Note the dispute with the Rafidah
The text explicitly says 'taking the fingers is not handshake, contrary to the Rafidah'. The Hanafi position requires full palm contact.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; both sides intend to follow the sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرقص والتصفيق، والصريخ وضرب الأوتار، والصنج والبوق الذي يفعله بعض من يدعي التصوف فإنه حرام بالإجماع لأنهازي الكفار كما في سكب الأنهر، وفي مجمع الأنهر عن التسهيل الوجد مراتب وبعضه يسلب الاختيار فلا وجه المطلق الإنكار وفي التتارخانية ما يدل على جوازه للمغلوب الذي حركاته كحركات المرتعش اهـ والمصافحة سنة في سائر الأوقات لما أخرج أبو داود عن أبي ذر : ما لقيت النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إلا وصافحني الحديث، وفيه اعتنقه مرة وفي القهستاني وغيره هي إلصاق الكف بالكف، وإقبال الوجه بالوجه، فأخذ الأصابع ليس بمصافحة خلافاً للروافض والسنة أن تكون بكلتا يديه وبغير حائل من نحو ثوب وعند اللقاء بعد السلام، وأن يأخذ الإيهام فإن فيه عرقاً تتشعب منه المحبة
And as for dancing, clapping, screaming, string instruments, cymbals, and trumpet that some who claim Sufism do, it is forbidden by consensus because it is imitation of disbelievers, as in Sakk al-Anhur. And in Majma' al-Anhur from al-Tashil: ecstatic states (wajd) have levels; some of it removes choice, so there is no absolute denial. And in al-Tatarkhaniyyah, there is indication of permissibility for the overwhelmed one whose movements are like those of a trembling person. End. And handshake (musafahah) is sunnah at all times due to what Abu Dawood narrated from Abu Dharr: 'I never met the Prophet except that he shook hands with me'—the hadith, and in it he embraced him once. In al-Qahistani and others, it is pressing palm to palm and facing face to face, so taking the fingers is not handshake, contrary to the Rafidah. And the sunnah is to do it with both hands and without a barrier like a cloth, and at the meeting after greeting, and to take the thumb, for in it is a vein from which love branches.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
Lesson Type: Ruling statement with proof. The text covers two topics. (1) Actions like dancing, clapping, screaming, and playing musical instruments (string instruments, cymbals, trumpet) are unanimously forbidden (haram) because they imitate the practices of disbelievers. However, the text notes that ecstatic states (wajd) may overwhelm a person to the point of losing control—in such cases, some scholars allow it based on necessity, likening the movements to those of a trembling person. (2) The handshake (musafahah) is a confirmed sunnah at all times, supported by the hadith of Abu Dharr. The correct form is pressing palm to palm and facing face to face; merely touching fingers is not considered a musafahah (opposing the Rafidah). It is sunnah to use both hands, without a barrier, at the time of meeting after greeting, and to clasp the thumb because it contains a vein that spreads love.

Key Terms
المصافحة (al-musafahah): Handshake: pressing palm to palm with face to face; a sunnah act.
الوجد (al-wajd): Ecstatic state in Sufi practice that may remove volition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on handshake (musafahah) according to the text?
A) Makruh
B) Sunnah at all times
C) Wajib
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
Dancing, musical instruments, clapping, etc. are haram by consensus due to imitation of disbelievers, but overwhelming ecstasy may excuse. Handshake is sunnah with both hands, palm to palm, clasping thumbs.
