Kissing, Embracing, and Greeting Customs: Hanafi Rulings and Dispute
Rulings on kissing, embracing, standing, bowing as greeting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on kissing and embracing another man, identify the grounds for disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, and apply the conditions that change the ruling.

Core Idea
Kissing another man in the Hanafi school: makruh according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but permissible for respect according to Abu Yusuf, based on Prophetic examples.

In al-Hidayah, the Hanafi school rules that it is disliked for a man to kiss another man's mouth or hand or embrace him in a single garment. However, Abu Yusuf dissents, allowing such actions for benevolence and honor. This lesson examines the evidence for each position and the conditions that determine the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling and the dissenting opinion
Extract the primary ruling from al-Hidayah (makruh) and the opposing view of Abu Yusuf (la ba'sa). Note the issue: kissing another man's mouth, hand, or any part, and embracing in one garment.
The ruling is about same-gender contact; not about spouses or mahram.
2
Analyze the Prophetic evidence cited by Abu Yusuf
Review the ahadith mentioned by Badr al-Ayni: the Prophet kissing his own hand, his family members, and deceased companions. These indicate permissibility for respect (mabarrat). Understand how they are used to support Abu Yusuf's view.
The ahadith are paraphrased; full chains are not given.
3
Determine the conditions for permissibility
Identify key conditions: intention (respect vs. desire) and clothing (additional garments beyond the izaar). For kissing, the ruling also depends on the status of the person (scholar, just ruler, etc.). For embracing, the disagreement is mitigated when both wear extra clothing.
See the conditions mentioned from various commentators.
4
Apply the ruling to modern contexts
Consider whether common greetings like hugging or hand-kissing fall under the dispute. Note that later Hanafi practice often follows Abu Yusuf for respect-based actions, especially when done to avoid discord.
Ibn Wahban recommends standing up in our time to avoid hatred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الهداية ويكره أن يقبل الرجل فم الرجل أو يده، أو شيئاً منه، أو يعانقه في إزار واحد، وقال أبو يوسف : لا بأس بذلك كله اهـ وفي غاية البيان عن الواقعات تقبيل يد العالم أو السلطان العادل جائز وورد في أحاديث ذكرها البدر العيني ما يفيد أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يقبل يده ورجله وكان يقبل الحسن وفاطمة، وقبل صلى الله عليه وسلم عثمان بن مظعون بعد موته، وكذلك قبل الصديق رضي الله تعالى عنه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بعد موته، وقبل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ابن عمه جعفراً بين عينيه، ثم قال البدر العيني: فعلم من مجموع ما ذكرنا إباحة تقبيل اليد والرجل، والكشح والرأس والجبهة والشفتين وبين العينين، ولكن كل ذلك إذا كان على وجه المبرة، والإكرام، وأما إذا كان ذلك على وجه الشهوة فلا يجوز إلا في حق الزوجين اهـ أي والسيد وأمته وفي رفع العوائق عن البحر الزاخر لا بأس بتقبيل يد العالم، والسلطان العادل، وفي غيرهما إن أراد شيئاً من عرض الدنيا فمكروه وإن أراد تعظيم المسلم، وإكرامه فلا بأس به اهـ وكان عمر يأخذ المصحف كل غداة، ويقبله وكان عثمان يقبله، ويمسحه على وجهه، وتقبيل الخبز قال أصحاب الشافعي رضي الله تعالى عنه : إنه بدعة مباحة، وقالوا: يكره بوسه وقواعدنا لا تأباه وفي رسالة المصافحة للشرنبلالي عن شيخ مشايخه الحانوتي : التحية بالركوع واسترخاء الرأس مكروهة لكل أحد مطلقاً، ومثله السلام باليد كما نصت عليه الحنفية اهـ قال الشرنبلالي : بعد ومحل كراهة الإشارة باليد إذا اقتصر عليها وذكر حديثاً يفيد أنه جمع بين اللفظ والإشارة، وفي شرح الوهبانية لابن الشحنة، وفي مشكل الآثار القيام لغيره ليس بمكروه لعينه إنما المكروه محبة القيام من الذي يقام له فإن لم يحب وقاموا له لا يكره لهم يعني جميعاً، قال : وقال القاضي البديع وقيام قارىء القرآن للقادم تعظيماً لا يكره إذا كان ممن يستحق التعظيم وقيل له أن يقوم بين يدي العالم تعظيماً له أما في غيره فلا يجوز، وقال ابن وهبان في شرحه والقيام يستحب في زماننا لما يورث تركه من والبغضاء والوعيد إنما هو في حق من يحب القيام بين يديه كما يفعله الترك، وفي أبي هريرة : كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يجلس معنا في المسجد يحدثنا فإذا قام قمنا قياماً حتى نراه قد دخل بعض بيوت أزواجه، وعن واثلة دخل رجل إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وهو قاعد في المسجد فتزحزح له رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال الرجل : يا رسول الله إن في المكان سعة فقال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : إن للمسلم لحقاً رواهما البيهقي في الشعب وأما المعانقة وهي كما في القهستاني جعل كل منهما يده على عنق الآخر فقالا بكراهتها وأباحه أبو يوسف، وظاهر عبارة مواهب الرحمن اختياره حيث قال مقتصراً عليه ويبيح أي أبو يوسف للرجل معانقة مثله وتقبيله للمبرة بلا شهوة كالمصافحة، وتقبيل يد العالم والسلطان العادل للتبرك اهـ قالوا الخلاف فيما إذا لم يكن عليهما غير الإزار ، وأما إذا كان عليهما قميص أو جبة أو رداء مع الإزار، فلا بأس به بالإجماع، كما في رفع العوائق عن الشمني والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم، وأستغفر الله العظيم.
And in al-Hidayah: It is disliked for a man to kiss a man's mouth or hand or any part of him, or to embrace him in one garment. Abu Yusuf said: All that is not objectionable. End. In Ghayat al-Bayan from al-Waqi'at: Kissing the hand of a scholar or a just ruler is permissible. Ahadith mentioned by Badr al-Ayni indicate that the Prophet used to kiss his own hand and foot, and he used to kiss Hasan and Fatima, and he kissed Uthman ibn Maz'un after his death, and similarly Abu Bakr kissed the Messenger of Allah after his death, and the Messenger of Allah kissed his cousin Ja'far between his eyes. Then Badr al-Ayni said: From the totality of what we mentioned, it is known that kissing the hand, foot, side, head, forehead, lips, and between the eyes is permissible, but all that is for benevolence and honor. If it is for desire, it is not permissible except for spouses—meaning also for master and his slave-girl. And in Raf' al-Awa'iq from al-Bahr al-Zakhir: There is no harm in kissing the hand of a scholar and a just ruler, and regarding others—if one intends something of the world's goods, it is disliked; but if one intends to honor and respect a Muslim, there is no harm. Umar used to take the Mushaf every morning and kiss it, and Uthman used to kiss it and wipe his face with it. As for kissing bread: The Shafi'is said it is an allowable innovation (bid'ah mubahah), and some said it is disliked; our principles do not reject it. In Risalah al-Musafahah by al-Shurunbulali from his teacher al-Hanuti: Greeting by bowing and relaxing the head is absolutely disliked for everyone, and similarly greeting by hand (gesture) as the Hanafis have stated. Al-Shurunbulali said: The disliked nature of gesturing by hand applies when one limits himself to it; he mentioned a hadith that indicates he combined speech and gesture. In Sharh al-Wahbaniyyah by Ibn al-Shihnah, and in Mushkil al-Athar: Standing for someone else is not disliked in itself; rather, what is disliked is the person's love of people standing for him. If he does not like it and they stand, it is not disliked for them—meaning all. He said: Al-Qadi al-Badi' said: Standing for a Quran reciter for the arriving person as a sign of respect is not disliked if the reciter deserves respect; it is said that one may stand before a scholar out of respect, but not for others. Ibn Wahban said in his commentary: Standing is recommended in our time because its abandonment leads to hatred and enmity; the threat is only for the one who loves people standing before him, like the Turks do. And from Abu Hurayrah: The Messenger of Allah used to sit with us in the mosque talking; when he stood, we would stand until we saw he had entered one of his wives' houses. From Wathilah: A man entered upon the Messenger of Allah while he was sitting in the mosque; the Messenger of Allah moved over for him. The man said: O Messenger of Allah, there is space in the place. The Prophet said: 'A Muslim has a right'—both narrated by al-Bayhaqi in al-Shu'ab. As for embracing, as in al-Qahistani: each placing his hand on the other's neck—they (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) said it is disliked, but Abu Yusuf allowed it. The apparent wording of Mawahib al-Rahman indicates a choice: he said, limiting himself, that Abu Yusuf permits a man to embrace another like him and kiss him for benevolence without desire, like handshake, and kissing the hand of a scholar and a just ruler for blessing. They said: The disagreement is when they have only an izaar. If they have a shirt or a cloak or a robe over the izaar, there is no objection by consensus, as in Raf' al-Awa'iq from al-Shumnuniy. And Allah knows best, and I seek forgiveness from Allah the Great.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
Lesson Type: Intra-Hanafi dispute with compilation of rulings. The text centers on a ruling from al-Hidayah that it is disliked (makruh) for a man to kiss another man's mouth, hand, or any part, or to embrace him in a single garment. Abu Yusuf disagrees, allowing all that. The author then cites various ahadith from Badr al-Ayni showing the Prophet's actions—kissing his own hand, his family members, and the deceased—which indicate permissibility for respect. The rule is: kissing for honor is allowed; for desire, only between spouses. The text covers other related practices: kissing the Quran, bread (dispute with Shafi'is), greeting by bowing or hand gesture (makruh), standing for someone (not disliked in itself, but disliked if the person loves it). For embracing, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad dislike it, but Abu Yusuf allows it for benevolence, especially when both wear extra clothing beyond the izaar. The relied-upon view is not explicitly stated, but the commentary shows the diversity of opinions within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
المعانقة (al-mu'anaqah): Embracing: each placing hand on the other's neck. Disputed ruling among Hanafi imams.
القيام (al-qiyam): Standing up for someone as a sign of respect; not disliked in itself, but disliked if the one standing desires it.
التحية بالركوع (al-tahiyyah bi al-ruku'): Greeting by bowing (like ruku' in prayer) or relaxing the head; absolutely disliked.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, what is the ruling on a man embracing another man with only an izaar?
A) Absolutely prohibited
B) Disliked
C) Not objectionable (la ba'sa)
D) Recommended

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the ruling on kissing and embracing another man depends on intention and context: it is disliked per Abu Hanifa and Muhammad but permitted for respect per Abu Yusuf, with later practice often adopting the permissive view.

Definition of Fasad and Butlan in Worship and Transactions
Introduction to the chapter on invalidators of prayer, defining two key terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fasid and batil in Hanafi fiqh, applying the correct term to examples in worship and transactions.

Core Idea
In Hanafi, fasid and batil differ in transactions but are identical in worship.

This lesson opens the chapter on what invalidates prayer by clarifying two critical terms: fasid (corrupt) and batil (void). While they are synonymous in worship, they have distinct meanings in transactions. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly classifying defective contracts and acts of worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the lexical root of ‘fasada’.
The text gives the verb forms to show that ‘fasada’ is a correct Arabic verb, and the form ‘infasada’ is not used. This establishes the proper derivation.
The lexical note from Al-Qamus is meant to confirm the word's usage.
2
Recognize the core distinction.
The author states that the terms are 'mufattaqan' (differing) in the context of fiqh. The key is: fasid is something legislated in its essence but not in its description; batil is not legislated in either.
This is the central legal principle for classifying transactions.
3
Apply the distinction to examples.
For transactions, a sale with an invalid condition is fasid (essence valid, condition defective). Selling a carcass or blood is batil (neither essence nor description valid). For worship, both terms mean invalid.
These examples illustrate the practical application.
4
Contrast with other schools.
Note that Shafi'i and Maliki treat fasid and batil as synonyms in all areas. This difference changes how defective contracts are handled.
The cross-madhhab comparison highlights the uniqueness of the Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يفسد الصلاة
المشكاة
عن
يقال : فسد كنصر، وعقد وكرم ولم يسمع انفسد قاموس ملخصاً. قوله: (مفترقان) فما كان مشروعاً بأصله دون وصفه كالبيع بشرط لا يقتضيه العقد فهو فاسد وما ليس مشروعاً
بأصله، ولا وصفه كبيع الميتة، والدم فهو باطل.
Chapter on what invalidates prayer. Al-Mishkat: It is said: fasada (to be corrupt) like nasara, and 'aqida, and karuma; 'infasada' is not heard. From Qamus summarized. His statement: (they differ): What is legislated in its essence but not in its description, like a sale with a condition not required by the contract, is fasid (corrupt). And what is not legislated in its essence nor its description, like selling a carcass or blood, is batil (void).
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
Lesson Type: Definition ruling. The text begins a new chapter 'Chapter on what invalidates prayer'. It first gives the lexical definition of 'fasada' (to be corrupt). Then it comments on the author's use of 'they differ' (مفترقان) meaning that the terms 'fasid' and 'batil' are not synonymous in all contexts. The key distinction: In transactions (mu'amalat), fasid refers to an act that is valid in its essence but defective in a description (e.g., a sale with an invalid condition). Batil refers to something not valid in its essence at all (e.g., selling a carcass). However, in worship (ibadat), fasid and batil are the same—both render the act invalid. This distinction is foundational in Hanafi fiqh, especially when discussing invalidators of prayer.

Key Terms
الفساد (al-fasad): In worship: synonymous with butlan (invalid). In transactions: a contract valid in essence but defective in a condition not required by the contract.
البطلان (al-butlan): In worship and transactions: completely void in essence and description.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition, how do fasad and butlan relate to each other in the context of transactions?
A) They are synonymous
B) Fasid is less severe than butlan
C) Butlan applies to essence, fasad applies to description
D) Fasad applies to worship only

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, fasid and batil are distinct in transactions: fasid is valid in essence but defective in description, while batil is void in both; in worship, they are synonymous.

Speech Invalidates Prayer: The Hanafi View and Refutation of Shafi'i
Establishing that speech invalidates prayer, including by mistake, and refuting Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on speech in prayer, identify the key hadith and its implication, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the concept of abrogation.

Core Idea
Any speech in prayer invalidates it—even forgetful or mistaken—because the prohibition is general and remains absolute.

This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi addresses a fundamental rule of prayer: speech invalidates it. It not only states the Hanafi position but defends it against the Shafi'i view that forgetfulness excuses. Understanding this dispute clarifies how the Hanafi school approaches textual evidence and the concept of abrogation (naskh). The commentary also clarifies the nuanced treatment of salam (greeting) when uttered forgetfully.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The passage states that any human speech, whether meaningful (like 'Ya') or not, even if uttered forgetfully or mistakenly, invalidates prayer. The only exception is salam when intended as dhikr.
The ruling is harsh to preserve the sanctity and focus of prayer.
2
Locate the Hanafi dalil
The primary proof is the hadith 'Inna hadhihi al-salata la yasluhu fiha shay'un min kalami al-nas' (This prayer is not suitable for any speech of the people). The word 'la yasluhu' indicates absolute prohibition.
The Hanafis treat this as a general, binding text.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that forgetful speech is excused, citing the hadith of Dhu al-Yadain where the Prophet spoke during prayer due to forgetfulness and later completed his prayer without repeating it.
This hadith appears to contradict the general prohibition.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is twofold: (1) The hadith of Dhu al-Yadain is abrogated (mansukh) because it occurred before the prohibition of speech. (2) If forgetfulness were an excuse, then little and much speech would be equal, but the Shafi'i themselves distinguish by length. Also, the general proof 'la yasluhu' cannot be overridden by a specific, earlier incident.
The Hanafis also use a qiyas: just as prayer is invalid without purity (a right of prayer), it is invalid with speech.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, a Hanafi must repeat his prayer if any speech occurs, while a Shafi'i is excused if forgetful (unless lengthy). This difference affects daily congregational and individual prayers.
The Hanafi position is more precautionary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يفسد الصلاة المشكاة عن (الكلمة) وإن لم تكن مفيدة كيا (ولو) نطق بها (سهواً) يظن كونه ليس في الصلاة (أو) نطق بها (خطأ) كما لو أراد أن يقول يا أيها الناس فقال يا يزيد ولو جهل كونه مفسداً، ولو نائماً في المختار لقوله إن هذه الصلاة لا يصلح فيها شيء من كلام الناس والعمل القليل عفو لعدم الاحتراز عنه (و) يفسدها (الدعاء بما يشبه (كلامنا) نحو اللهم ألبسني ثوب الصلاة وتمثيله قوله : ( منه الكلمة) ويشترط فيها تصحيح الحروف وسماعها حتى تكون مفسدة فإن فقد أحدهما فلا فساد لأنه لا يعتبر كلاماً اهـ حلبي . قوله : ( وإن لم تكن مفيدة كيا) ذكر الإمام خواهر زاده أنها تفسد بالنفخ المسموع بلا حروف، وفي صوت لا هجاء له وإن كره بأنه عمل كثير يظن من رأى فاعله أنه ليس في لغير المفيدة بيا فيه نظر فإنها بمعنى أدعو فهي نائبة عن جملة وأما المنادي فهو فضلة لأنه مفعول في المعنى وقد تأتي للتنبيه اللهم إلا أن يقال عده لها غير مفيدة نظر إلى عدم تعيين المنادى، واعلم أنه لا فرق في المفسد إذا كان حرفين بين أن يكون من أحرف الزيادة، أو لا وفصل أبو يوسف وتفصيل المقام يعلم من المطولات. قوله : (ولو) نطق بها سهواً) الفرق بين السهو والنسيان أن الصورة الحاصلة عند العقل إن كان يمكنه الملاحظة أي وقت شاء تسمى ذهولاً وسهواً، ولا أي لا يمكنه الملاحظة إلا بعد كسب جديد تسمى نسياناً، نهر وبينه وبين الخطأ أن السهو ما يتنبه له صاحبه والخطأ ما لم يتنبه له بالتنبيه أو يتنبه بعد أتعاب حموي عن الأكمل، وقال الإمام الشافعي رضي الله عنه : لا تفسد بالكلام ناسياً إلا إذا طال، واحتج بحديث ذي اليدين ولنا قوله : وليبن على صلاته ما مع وجود الكلام، وهو واضح جداً، ولو كان النسيان عذراً لاستوى قليله وكثيره وحديث ذي اليدين كان في ابتداء الإسلام قبل تحريم الكلام فإن قيل : السلام كالكلام في أن كلا منهما قاطع للصلاة فلم فصلتم في السلام بين العمد والنسيان، فالجواب أن السلام له شبه بالأذكار إذ هو من أسماء الله تعالى، ومذكور في التشهد فهو من جنس الصلاة، وإنما يلحق بالكلام إذا قصد به الخطاب فإذا أتى به ناسياً اعتبرناه بالأذكار وإن كان عمداً اعتبرناه بالكلام عملاً بالشبهين .اهـ. قوله : ( في المختار) واختار فخر الإسلام وغيره أنها لا تفسد كما في المضمرات والمنح قوله : (لا يصلح فيها شيء الخ) كذا في رواية الإمام أحمد، ومسلم والنسائي، وفي رواية أبي داود والطبراني لا يحل مكان لا يصلح. قال في الشرح : وما لا يحل ولا يصلح في الصلاة فمباشرته تفسدها .اهـ. قوله : (والعمل القليل عفو هذا جواب عن حاشية الطحطاوي/ م٢١
Chapter of what invalidates prayer. Al-Mishkat: (The word) even if it is not meaningful like 'Ya', (even if) he utters it forgetfully, thinking he is not in prayer, (or) he utters it mistakenly, as if he wanted to say 'O people' and said 'O Yazid', even if he is ignorant that it invalidates, even if asleep, in al-Mukhtar because of his saying: 'This prayer is not suitable for any speech of the people', and small actions are excused because it is impossible to avoid them. And (it is invalidated by) supplication that resembles our speech, like 'O Allah, clothe me with the garment of prayer' and its example. His saying: (from it is the word) and it is required that the letters are pronounced correctly and heard for it to be invalidating; if either is missing, there is no invalidation because it is not considered speech. Al-Halabi. His saying: (even if not meaningful like Ya) Imam Khawarizmi said it invalidates by blowing if heard without letters. In al-Siraj and al-Binaya: If he speaks speech that is customary in people's understanding, whether it contains letters or not, even if he says something like what a donkey is urged with, his prayer is invalidated. Hence al-Shurunbulali found problematic what some mentioned that if one urges a donkey it does not invalidate because it is a sound without letters, although disliked because it is a lot of action and the observer thinks he is not in prayer. Regarding the non-meaningful 'Ya', there is consideration: it means 'I call' so it stands for a sentence, and the vocative is extra. It may come for attention. Unless it is said that it was counted as non-meaningful due to lack of specifying the called person. Know that there is no difference in invalidator if it is two letters, whether from the letters of addition or not; Abu Yusuf made a distinction, and the details of the matter are known from the longer works. His saying: (even if he utters it forgetfully) The difference between forgetfulness (sahw) and forgetfulness (nisyan) is that the image present in the mind if it can be noticed at any time is called heedlessness and sahw, and if it cannot be noticed except after new effort, it is called nisyan. Al-Nahr. And between it and error (khata') is that sahw is what the person is alert to, and khata' is what he is not alert to even after reminder, or after difficulty. From al-Hamawi quoting al-Akmal. Imam al-Shafi'i said: It is not invalidated by speech when forgetful unless it is lengthy, and he argued with the hadith of Dhu al-Yadain. Our proof is his saying: 'Let him build upon his prayer as long as he does not speak', where he made the permissibility of building conditioned by not speaking, so this implies the end of permissibility with speech, and the generality of his saying: 'This prayer is not suitable for speech' indicates that the absence of speech is a right of prayer, just as the presence of purity is its right. So as prayer is not valid without purity, it is not valid with speech, and this is very clear. If forgetfulness were an excuse, then little and much would be equal. The hadith of Dhu al-Yadain was in the beginning of Islam before the prohibition of speech. If it is said: The greeting of salam is like speech in that both cut the prayer, so why do you differentiate in salam between intentional and forgetful? The answer: Salam resembles remembrances because it is one of Allah's names and mentioned in tashahhud, so it is of the same type as prayer. It is only considered speech if intended as address. If one says it forgetfully, we consider it as remembrance; if intentionally, we consider it as speech, acting on both resemblances. End. His saying: (in al-Mukhtar) Fakhr al-Islam and others chose that it does not invalidate, as in al-Mudmarat and al-Mannah. His saying: ('Nothing is suitable in it') is the narration of Imam Ahmad, Muslim, and al-Nasa'i. In the narration of Abu Dawud and al-Tabarani: 'Nothing is permissible' instead of 'suitable'. He said in the commentary: Whatever is not permissible and not suitable in prayer, its commission invalidates it. End. His saying: (And small actions are excused) This is an answer to the commentary on al-Tahtawi. End.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a detailed inter-madhhab dispute about speech invalidating prayer, with the Hanafi position against al-Shafi'i. The commentator (al-Tahtawi) explains the rulings on speech in prayer: (1) Any word, even if not meaningful like 'Ya', invalidates if it consists of two letters and is audible. (2) This applies even if uttered forgetfully, mistakenly, or out of ignorance. (3) The Hanafi position is based on the hadith: 'This prayer is not suitable for any speech of the people', which is a general prohibition. (4) Al-Shafi'i argued that speech when forgetful does not invalidate, citing the story of Dhu al-Yadain where the Prophet spoke during prayer due to forgetfulness. The Hanafi response: That incident occurred before the prohibition of speech was revealed; it is abrogated. (5) The commentator also clarifies the difference between سهو (sahw) and نسيان (nisyan): sahw is a lapse of attention that one can recall easily, nisyan is a complete forgetting. However, both invalidate prayer if speech occurs. (6) A key objection is raised: If salam (greeting) is like speech, why is it treated differently? The answer: Salam has a dual nature—it is a remembrance of Allah (an act of prayer) when one intends it as dhikr, but it is considered speech if addressed to someone intentionally. If uttered forgetfully, it is treated as dhikr and does not invalidate. This shows the Hanafi method of resolving apparent contradictions by considering the predominant aspect. (7) The passage ends by noting that small actions (like slight movements) are excused, but major action invalidates.

Key Terms
سهو (sahw): A lapse of attention where the person can recall the correct state upon noticing. Used for forgetfulness in prayer.
نسيان (nisyan): Complete forgetting, where the person cannot recall without new effort. In prayer, speech due to nisyan invalidates according to Hanafis.
خطأ (khata'): Error: the person intended something else but said the wrong word. Hanafis: invalidates because the spoken word is still speech.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How do Hanafis respond to the Shafi'i argument that speech during prayer is excused when forgetful, based on the hadith of Dhu al-Yadain?
A) They accept the hadith as abrogated
B) They say the hadith is weak
C) They argue that forgetfulness does not excuse because the prohibition is general
D) Both A and C

Key Takeaway
Any human speech during prayer invalidates it, and forgetfulness or mistake does not excuse; the hadith of Dhu al-Yadain is abrogated.

Conditions for Speech Nullifying Prayer
Ruling with conditions for speech nullifying prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for a word to nullify prayer and identify whether a given utterance meets them.

Core Idea
A word nullifies prayer only if its letters are correct and audible.

The Hanafi school holds that any speech during prayer nullifies it, but there are precise conditions for what counts as speech. This lesson clarifies those conditions and provides examples, as well as the distinction between forgetfulness (sahw) and oversight (nisyan).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
The act is speaking during prayer. The default ruling is that it nullifies the prayer, based on the general prohibition.
2
List the conditions for nullification
From the text: the word must have correct letters (huruf) and be heard (masmu'). If either is missing, it is not considered speech and does not nullify.
The condition of audibility ensures that only uttered speech counts.
3
Apply conditions to examples
Apply to the examples given: meaningless 'ya', blowing without letters, customary speech like donkey-driving sounds. Determine which nullify based on conditions.
Even meaningless words and sounds with letters nullify; blowing without letters is disputed.
4
Distinguish between sahw and nisyan
Sahw (forgetfulness) is when the person can recall at will; nisyan (oversight) requires new cognitive effort. Both are treated as nullifying, but this distinction affects other rulings.
In Hanafi, forgetful speech still nullifies, unlike Shafi'i.
5
Note the exception for the sleeping person
The chosen view (mukhtar) is that the sleeping person's speech does not nullify, as chosen by Fakhr al-Islam and others. This is an exception because the person is not conscious.
This is found in al-Mudmarat and al-Minh.

قوله : ( منه الكلمة ) ويشترط فيها تصحيح الحروف وسماعها حتى تكون مفسدة فإن فقد أحدهما فلا فساد لأنه لا يعتبر كلاماً اهـ حلبي . قوله : ( وإن لم تكن مفيدة كيا ) ذكر الإمام خواهر زاده أنها تفسد بالنفخ المسموع بلا حروف، وفي السراج والبناية : إذا تكلم كلاماً يتعارف في متفاهم الناس سواء حصل به حروف أو لا حتى لو قال ما يساق به الحمار مثلاً فسدت صلاته اهـ ومن ثمة استشكل الشرنبلالي ما ذكره بعضهم من أنه ساق حماراً لم تفسد لأنه صوت لا هجاء له وإن كره بأنه عمل كثير يظن من رأى فاعله أنه ليس في الصلاة. قوله : ( ولو ) نطق بها سهواً ) الفرق بين السهو والنسيان أن الصورة الحاصلة عند العقل إن كان يمكنه الملاحظة أي وقت شاء تسمى ذهولاً وسهواً، ولا أي لا يمكنه الملاحظة إلا بعد كسب جديد تسمى نسياناً، نهر وبينه وبين الخطأ أن السهو ما يتنبه له صاحبه والخطأ ما لم يتنبه له بالتنبيه أو يتنبه بعد أتعاب حموي عن الأكمل، وقال الإمام الشافعي رضي الله عنه : لا تفسد بالكلام ناسياً إلا إذا طال، واحتج بحديث ذي اليدين ولنا قوله : وليبن على صلاته ما لم يتكلم حيث غيا جواز البناء بالتكلم فيقتضي إنتهاء الجواز بالتكلم وعموم قوله : إن هذه الصلاة لا يصلح فيها شيء من كلام الناس دل على أن عدم الكلام من حقها كما جعل وجود الطهارة من حقها فكما لا تجوز مع عدم الطهارة لا تجوز مع وجود الكلام، وهو واضح جداً، ولو كان النسيان عذراً لاستوى قليله وكثيره وحديث ذي اليدين كان في ابتداء الإسلام قبل تحريم الكلام فإن قيل : السلام كالكلام في أن كلا منهما قاطع للصلاة فلم فصلتم في السلام بين العمد والنسيان، فالجواب أن السلام له شبه بالأذكار إذ هو من أسماء الله تعالى، ومذكور في التشهد فهو من جنس الصلاة، وإنما يلحق بالكلام إذا قصد به الخطاب فإذا أتى به ناسياً اعتبرناه بالأذكار وإن كان عمداً اعتبرناه بالكلام عملاً بالشبهين .اهـ. قوله : ( في المختار ) واختار فخر الإسلام وغيره أنها لا تفسد كما في المضمرات والمنح قوله : ( لا يصلح فيها شيء الخ ) كذا في رواية الإمام أحمد، ومسلم والنسائي، وفي رواية أبي داود والطبراني لا يحل مكان لا يصلح. قال في الشرح : وما لا يحل ولا يصلح في الصلاة فمباشرته تفسدها .اهـ.
His saying: (from it, the word). The condition for it being nullifying is that the letters are pronounced correctly and heard; if either is missing, it is not nullifying because it is not considered speech. End quote from Halabi. His saying: (even if it is not meaningful, like 'ya'). Imam Khawarazmi mentioned that it nullifies with audible blowing without letters. In al-Siraj and al-Binaya: if one speaks speech that is customary among people, whether letters are produced or not, even if one says something like what is used to drive a donkey, his prayer is nullified. End quote. Therefore, al-Shurunbulali objected to what some mentioned that if one says 'saq-himaran', it does not nullify because it is a sound without spelling, even if disliked, because it is much action that makes an onlooker think he is not in prayer. His saying: (and even if he says it forgetfully). The difference between forgetfulness (sahw) and oversight (nisyan) is that if the mental image can be observed at will, it is called ghafla and sahw; if it cannot be observed except with new effort, it is called nisyan. Al-Nahr. The difference between sahw and error (khata) is that sahw is what one is alerted to, while khata is what one is not alerted to even with prompting, or is alerted to after effort. Al-Hamawi from al-Akmal. Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, said: Speech does not nullify if one is forgetful unless it is lengthy, and he argued with the hadith of Dhu al-Yadain. Our proof: his statement: 'Let him build upon his prayer as long as he does not speak,' making the permissibility of building contingent upon not speaking, so the permissibility ends with speaking. And the general meaning of his statement: 'Indeed this prayer is not fitting for any speech of people' indicates that absence of speech is a right of prayer, just as presence of purity is its right. So just as prayer is not valid with absence of purity, it is not valid with presence of speech. This is very clear. If forgetfulness were an excuse, then little and much would be equal. The hadith of Dhu al-Yadain was at the beginning of Islam before the prohibition of speech. If it is said: greeting (salam) is like speech in that both cut off prayer, so why did you differentiate in greeting between intentional and forgetful? The answer is that greeting resembles dhikr because it is a name of Allah and mentioned in the tashahhud, so it is of the same type as prayer. It is only considered speech when intended for address. Thus if one does it forgetfully, we consider it as dhikr; if intentionally, we consider it as speech, acting on both similarities. End quote. His saying: (in the chosen view). Fakhr al-Islam and others chose that it does not nullify, as in al-Mudmarat and al-Minh. His saying: ('Nothing of it is fitting') is as in the narration of Imam Ahmad, Muslim, and al-Nasa'i. In the narration of Abu Dawud and al-Tabarani: 'It is not permissible' instead of 'is not fitting.' It is said in al-Sharh: Whatever is not permissible and not fitting in prayer, directly doing it nullifies it. End quote.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary on the ruling that speech nullifies prayer. The author clarifies that for a word to nullify, it must be correct in letters and audible; otherwise it is not considered speech. Even meaningless sounds like 'ya' or blowing without letters nullify, according to many authorities. The distinction between forgetfulness (sahw) and oversight (nisyan) is explained: in sahw, the person can recall at will; in nisyan, he cannot without new effort. Imam al-Shafi'i argued that forgetful speech does not nullify unless lengthy, citing the hadith of Dhu al-Yadain. The Hanafi response uses the hadith 'Let him build upon his prayer as long as he does not speak' to show that any speech ends the permissibility of building, and the general prohibition 'There is no part of people's speech in this prayer' indicates speech is incompatible with prayer. The Shafi'i hadith is abrogated. The question of why greeting is treated differently is answered: greeting resembles dhikr, so it is not like ordinary speech when done forgetfully. Finally, the preferred opinion is that the sleeping person's speech does not nullify, as chosen by Fakhr al-Islam.

Key Terms
الكلمة (al-kalimah): A word that nullifies prayer; requires correct pronunciation and audibility.
السهو (al-sahw): Forgetfulness that can be recalled at will without new effort.
النسيان (al-nisyan): Forgetfulness that cannot be recalled without acquiring new cognition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition for a word to nullify prayer according to the Hanafi school?
A) The word must consist of correctly pronounced letters.
B) The word must be heard by the speaker.
C) The word must be meaningful.
D) Both A and B are conditions.

Key Takeaway
A word nullifies prayer only if its letters are correct and audible; even meaningless utterances like 'ya' qualify.

Salah — Prayer
advanced
10 min
Hanafi Proof against Shafi'i on Forgetful Speech
Inter-madhhab dispute on forgetful speech nullifying prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi proof that any speech, even if forgetful, nullifies prayer, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The hadith 'Let him build upon his prayer as long as he does not speak' proves that any speaking ends the prayer's continuity.

Imam al-Shafi'i argued that forgetful speech does not nullify unless lengthy, citing the hadith of Dhu al-Yadain. The Hanafi school responds with stronger evidence and shows that the Shafi'i hadith is abrogated. This lesson examines the proofs and the special case of greeting (salam).

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i position and proof
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that forgetful speech does not nullify prayer unless it is lengthy. His proof is the hadith of Dhu al-Yadain, where the Prophet (peace be upon him) spoke to someone forgetfully and then completed the prayer.
2
State the Hanafi position and proof
The Hanafi school holds that any speech, intentional or forgetful, nullifies prayer. Their proof: the Prophet said, 'Let him build upon his prayer as long as he does not speak,' making the permissibility of building contingent on not speaking. Also: 'This prayer is not fitting for any speech of people.'
3
Analyze the Hanafi proofs
The first hadith (build upon prayer until he speaks) implies that speaking ends the option to continue, i.e., nullifies. The second hadith (no speech in prayer) is a general prohibition. Both are general and strong.
The Hanafi argue that if forgetfulness were an excuse, the Prophet would have made an exception for little speech.
4
Address the Shafi'i hadith
The hadith of Dhu al-Yadain is abrogated because it occurred before the prohibition of speech in prayer. The general prohibition came later and abrogated the earlier permission.
5
Explain the exception for salam
Greeting (salam) is treated differently because it resembles dhikr; it is a name of Allah and is part of the tashahhud. Intentionally it is speech, forgetfully it is like dhikr and does not nullify.
This reconciles the conflict: if salam were ordinary speech, it would nullify even forgetfully. But due to its resemblance to dhikr, it is an exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

السلام (al-salam): The greeting 'Assalamu alaykum', considered a form of dhikr in prayer.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate

Nullifiers of Prayer: Du'a Resembling Human Speech, Greeting, and Excessive Action
Ruling on du'a, greeting, and action in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between supplications that nullify prayer and those that do not based on the Hanafi criterion of attainability from people, and apply the same reasoning to greeting and excessive action.

Core Idea
A supplication that asks for something obtainable from people nullifies prayer; only purely divine requests are permitted.

In the Hanafi school, not all supplications in prayer are allowed. The key is whether the request could be fulfilled by a human being. Similarly, greeting and excessive movement nullify prayer. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind these rulings and the distinction between speech and action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of nullifiers
The passage lists three types of nullifiers: du'a resembling human speech, greeting (salam), and excessive action. Note that each has its own criterion.
2
Analyze the criterion for du'a
For du'a, the nullifying condition is that the request can be obtained from people (e.g., 'give me a garment'). Purely spiritual requests (e.g., 'grant me well-being') do not nullify.
This is the 'ala al-sahih' (correct) view, implying disagreement with other schools.
3
Analyze greeting and response
Initiating greeting with intention to greet nullifies, even if forgetful or without 'alaykum'. Responding verbally or by handshake also nullifies because it conveys meaning.
The text says 'because it is addressing'.
4
Analyze excessive action
Action that leaves no doubt to an observer that the person is not in prayer is 'much' and nullifies. If doubt remains, it is 'little' and does not nullify.
Little action is excused because the body naturally moves; speech is not excused because it can be avoided.
5
Understand the rationale for the speech-action distinction
The text explains that little speech is not excused because one can naturally avoid speaking, whereas little bodily movement is inevitable. This is why little speech nullifies but little action does not.
An example: eating forgetfully in fasting does not nullify, but in prayer it does, because prayer requires constant awareness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويفسدها الدعاء بما يشبه كلامنا نحو اللهم ألبسني ثوب كذا أو أطعمني كذا، أو اقض ديني أو ارزقني فلانة على الصحيح لأنه يمكن تحصيله من العباد بخلاف قوله اللهم عافني واعف عني وارزقني. (و) يفسدها السلام بنية التحية وإن لم يقل عليكم (ولو) كان (ساهياً) لأنه خطاب (و) يفسدها رد السلام بلسانه ولو سهواً (أو) رد السلام (بالمصافحة) لأنه كلام معنى (و) يفسدها العمل الكثير لا القليل والفاصل بينهما أن الكثير هو الذي لا يشك الناظر لفاعله أنه ليس في الصلاة، وإن اشتبه فهو قليل. سؤال حاصله انكم جعلتم الكلام قليله وكثيره مفسداً وفصلتم في العمل بين قليله فلا يفسد وكثيره فيفسد، وحاصل الجواب أنه إنما عفي عن القليل من العمل لأن بدن الحي لا يخلو عن حركة طبعاً فلا يمكن الاحتراز عن قليلها فعفي ما لم يكثر، ويدخل في حد ما لا يمكن الاحتراز عنه، وليس الكلام كذلك، فإن يمكن الاحتراز عن قليله لأنه ليس من طبعه أن يتكلم فلم يعف وعن نحو الأكل ناسياً في الصوم دون الصلاة لأن حالة الصلاة مذكرة دون الصوم اهـ.
And it (prayer) is nullified by supplication that resembles our speech, such as 'O Allah, clothe me with such and such garment' or 'feed me such and such', or 'pay my debt' or 'provide for me so-and-so' according to the correct view, because it can be obtained from people, unlike his saying 'O Allah, grant me well-being, pardon me, and provide for me.' And it is nullified by greeting (salam) with the intention of greeting, even if he does not say 'and upon you', and even if he is forgetful, because it is addressing. And it is nullified by responding to greeting with one's tongue, even forgetfully, or responding with a handshake, because it is speech in meaning. And it is nullified by much action, not little. The distinction between the two is that much action is that which an onlooker has no doubt that the doer is not in prayer; if there is doubt, it is little. A question: you have made speech, both little and much, a nullifier, but distinguished in action between little (not nullifying) and much (nullifying). The answer: little action is excused because a living body is not free from natural movement, so it cannot be avoided; therefore it is excused as long as it is not excessive. Speech is not like that, because its little can be avoided, as it is not natural to speak. Similarly, eating forgetfully in fasting (does not nullify) unlike in prayer, because the state of prayer reminds one, unlike fasting. End quote.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage covers three distinct nullifiers of prayer: du'a that resembles human speech, greeting (salam), and excessive action. For du'a, the key distinction is whether the request can be obtained from people. If one asks Allah for something that is normally sought from people (like 'give me a garment'), it nullifies because it resembles human speech; but if one asks for purely divine gifts (like 'grant me well-being'), it does not nullify. The text mentions a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who argues that du'a never nullifies. The Hanafi position distinguishes based on feasibility of human attainment. For greeting, intentional greeting with the intention of greeting nullifies even if forgetful; responding verbally or even by shaking hands (since it conveys meaning without words) also nullifies. For action, the criterion is whether an observer would doubt whether the person is in prayer: if no doubt it is much and nullifies; if doubt, it is little and excused. The text also answers why little speech is not excused like little action: speech can be avoided but natural body movements cannot. The ruling on eating forgetfully is given as an example: fasting does not nullify, but prayer does because prayer requires constant awareness.

Key Terms
الدعاء بما يشبه كلامنا (al-du'a bima yushbihu kalamina): Supplication that resembles human speech because the request can be obtained from people.
العمل الكثير (al-'amal al-kathir): Action so excessive that an onlooker would have no doubt the doer is not in prayer.
السلام (al-salam): Greeting (saying 'assalamu alaykum') with the intention of greeting, which nullifies prayer.
المصافحة (al-musafaha): Handshake, which nullifies prayer when done as a response to greeting because it conveys meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following supplications in prayer would nullify the prayer according to the Hanafi school?
A) 'O Allah, grant me well-being.'
B) 'O Allah, feed me such and such food.'
C) 'O Allah, forgive me and have mercy on me.'
D) 'O Allah, guide me to the straight path.'

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, supplications that ask for worldly things obtainable from people nullify prayer, while purely spiritual requests are permissible.

Salam and Reply in Prayer: Initiating and Responding
Ruling on initiating and replying to salam during prayer with proof from hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on initiating salam and replying to salam during prayer, and distinguish between actions that invalidate and those that are merely disliked.

Core Idea
Initiating salam or replying with speech invalidates prayer; replying with a gesture is valid but disliked.

Prayer is a state of devotion requiring focus on Allah. Interacting with others through speech or meaningful gestures can break that focus. This passage from al-Tahtawi clarifies exactly which types of salam invalidate the prayer and which are permissible but disliked, using a hadith of the Prophet (ﷺ) to draw the line.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three actions and their rulings
List the three cases: initiating salam, replying with tongue, replying with handshake. For each, note whether the prayer is invalidated or not.
Initiating salam and replying with tongue invalidate; replying with handshake also invalidates because it is 'meaningful speech'.
2
Locate the dalil for the gesture exception
The hadith of Bilal reports that the Prophet (ﷺ) gestured with his hand when greeted in prayer. This is the proof that replying with a hand gesture does not invalidate.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but qati al-dalala on permissibility.
3
Understand the principle of 'kalam ma'na'
Actions that convey meaning, like a handshake, are treated as speech in effect and thus invalidate the prayer. Simple gestures like waving do not convey the same level of communication.
The handshake is considered a form of speech because it directly reciprocates the greeting.
4
Resolve the apparent conflict: gesture is makruh tanzih
Though the Prophet's gesture shows permissibility, later scholars ruled it makruh tanzih for us. The Prophet's action was to teach the ruling, so it is not considered disliked for him. For the ordinary person, it is better to avoid gestures in prayer.
Ibn Amir Hajj explained that the karahah is tanzihi, not tahrimi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و يفسدها السلام بنية التحية وإن لم يقل عليكم ولو كان ساهياً لأنه خطاب. و يفسدها رد السلام بلسانه ولو سهواً أو رد السلام بالمصافحة لأنه كلام معنى. قوله: (بلسانه) قيد به لأنه لو رده بيده لا تفسد لما روي أن النبي خرج إلى قباء فجاء الأنصار، فسلموا عليه قال عمر: قلت لبلال: كيف النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حين كانوا يسلمون عليه، وهو يصلي؟ قال: يقول هكذا وبسط جعفر بن عوف كفه وجعل بطنه أسفل وظهره إلى فوق. فإن قلت: هذا يقتضي عدم الكراهة وقد صرحوا بكراهة الرّد بالإشارة، وهو في الصلاة، أجاب العلامة ابن أمير حاج بأنها كراهة تنزيه، وفعله إنما كان تعليماً للجواز فلا يوصف بالكرامة.
And it invalidates it: salam with the intention of greeting, even if he does not say 'alaykum', and even if forgetful, because it is speech. And replying to salam with his tongue invalidates, even if forgetful, or replying with a handshake, because it is meaningful speech. His statement: 'with his tongue' restricts it; because if he replies with his hand, it does not invalidate, due to the report that the Prophet (ﷺ) went out to Quba, and the Ansar greeted him. Umar said: I asked Bilal: how did the Prophet (ﷺ) respond when they greeted him while he was praying? He said: he did like this, and Ja'far ibn Awf spread his palm, making its inside down and back up. If you say: this implies no dislike, but they declared replying by gesture disliked in prayer; the scholar Ibn Amir Haj answered that it is a disliked tanzih, and his action was teaching permissibility, so it is not described as disliked (makruh).
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage presents rulings on salam in prayer. First, initiating salam (saying 'assalamu alaykum') with the intention of greeting another person invalidates the prayer, even if the person does not say 'alaykum' (the complete phrase) and even if done forgetfully, because it is speech addressed to a human. Second, replying to someone's salam with the tongue invalidates the prayer, even if forgetfully. Third, replying with a handshake also invalidates because it is 'meaningful speech' (kalam ma'na) – i.e., an action that conveys a message. However, replying with a hand gesture (like waving) does NOT invalidate the prayer, based on the hadith where the Prophet (ﷺ) gestured with his hand in response to salam while praying. The commentary clarifies that such gesturing is considered makruh tanzih (disliked but not sinful) for the one praying, not because it invalidates, but because it is not the best manner. The Prophet's action was to teach permissibility, so it is not considered disliked for him. The key distinction is between speaking (invalidates) and gesturing (does not invalidate, but is slightly disliked).

Key Terms
السلام بنية التحية (al-salam bi-niyyat al-tahiyyah): Saying 'assalamu alaykum' intending to greet a person, not as the closing of prayer.
كلام معنى (kalam ma'na): An action that conveys meaning, like a handshake, treated as speech in effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The prayer is invalidated
B) The prayer is valid but it is makruh tanzih
C) The prayer is valid and it is mustahabb
D) The prayer becomes batil and must be repeated

Key Takeaway
Initiating salam or replying with speech invalidates prayer; a hand gesture does not but is disliked.

Du'a in Prayer: When It Invalidates
Inter-madhhab dispute over du'a invalidation in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between permissible and invalidating supplications in prayer according to the Hanafi school, and explain the Hanafi-Shafi'i disagreement.

Core Idea
A supplication in prayer invalidates if it requests something possible from a human; otherwise it is valid.

This passage addresses a common question: can you make du'a in prayer? The answer depends on the content. The Hanafi school distinguishes between du'a that is worship and du'a that resembles human conversation. The Shafi'is disagree, holding that all du'a is permissible. Let's examine the Hanafi criterion and its evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: du'a that requests something possible from a human invalidates prayer; du'a from Qur'an, transmitted supplications, or requests impossible from humans do not.
The criterion is from al-Marghinani.
2
Identify the opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that du'a never invalidates prayer because it is worship, not ordinary speech.
This is the key point of dispute.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi response: the ruling is based on the content resembling human speech, not the addressee. Saying 'I recited al-Fatihah' invalidates even without an addressee.
The example shows that addressing is not required.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: a Hanafi must restrict du'a to Qur'anic/athar or general impossible requests; a Shafi'i can make any du'a without invalidation.
This affects daily practice.
5
Apply the criterion
Given a du'a, classify it: is it from Qur'an/athar? If yes, valid. If no, is the request impossible from humans? If yes, valid. Otherwise, it invalidates.
Use the flowchart from the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذا أو أطعمني كذا، أو اقض ديني أو ارزقني فلانة على الصحيح لأنه يمكن تحصيله من العباد بخلاف قوله اللهم عافني واعف عني وارزقني. تقدم أن هذا مما ورد في السنة، وذكر في البحر عن المرغيناني ضابطاً، فقال: الحاصل أنه إذا دعا في الصلاة بما جاء في القرآن، أو في المأثور لا تفسد صلاته وإن لم يكن في القرآن أو المأثور فإن استحال طلبه من العباد لا يفسد وإلا أفسد اهـ. ملخصاً من الشرح، فجعل التفصيل بين ما استحال، وما لم يستحل فيما لم يرد في القرآن، والسنة، وإنما خص الدعاء مع دخوله في عموم الكلام لوقوع الخلاف فيه فإن الإمام الشافعي الله عنه يقول بعدم الفساد به، فإن قيل : الدعاء ليس بخطاب الآدمي فكيف يكون من كلام الناس قلنا: لا يشترط في ذلك المخاطبة ألا ترى أن من قال: قرأت الفاتحة مثلاً تبطل صلاته وإن لم يكن بحضرته أحد يخاطبه كذا في التبيين. قوله : (أو ارزقني) أشار به إلى الفرق بين طلب الرزق المقيد بنحو فلان فيفسد والمطلق كهذا فلا يفسد.
Or 'feed me such-and-such', or 'pay my debt', or 'provide me with so-and-so (as a wife)' – correct view, because it can be obtained from human beings, unlike saying 'O Allah, grant me well-being, pardon me, and provide for me'. This was mentioned as from the Sunnah, and in al-Bahr from al-Marghinani as a criterion: the summary is that if one supplicates in prayer with something from the Qur'an or transmitted supplication, his prayer is not invalidated; if not from Qur'an or transmitted, then if requesting what is impossible from creatures, it does not invalidate; otherwise it does. This detail is for what is not in Qur'an and Sunnah; the specific mention of du'a amidst general speech is due to the disagreement: Imam al-Shafi'i holds it does not invalidate. If it is said: du'a is not addressing a human, so how is it from people's speech? We say: addressing is not required; do you not see that one who says 'I recited al-Fatihah' invalidates his prayer even if no one is present? Thus in al-Tabyin. His statement 'or provide me' indicates the difference between restricted request (like so-and-so) which invalidates, and unrestricted which does not.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute about du'a in prayer. The text states the Hanafi ruling: a supplication that requests something possible from a human being (e.g., 'feed me', 'pay my debt', 'marry me to so-and-so') invalidates the prayer. However, a general, unrestricted request from Allah alone (e.g., 'O Allah, provide for me') does not invalidate. The commentary then provides a criterion from al-Marghinani: if the du'a is from the Qur'an or transmitted (athar), it never invalidates. If it is not, then if the request is impossible from creatures (e.g., 'O Allah, forgive me'), it does not invalidate; if it is possible, it does. This distinguishes the Hanafi position from Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that du'a does not invalidate at all. The Hanafi respond by clarifying that the prohibition is on speech addressed to humans, not on du'a per se, but the defining factor is that the du'a resembles human request. The example of saying 'I recited al-Fatihah' (which is not addressed to anyone) shows that addressing is not required for invalidation; rather, the key is that it is speech from the human realm. The text also notes that 'arzuqni' (provide me) alone is general and does not invalidate, but if specified to a person it does.

Key Terms
الدعاء (al-du'a): Supplication to Allah, but in this context includes requests that may be directed to humans in form.
كلام الناس (kalam al-nas): Speech of humans, i.e., ordinary conversation, which invalidates prayer.
المأثور (al-ma'thur): Transmitted supplications from the Prophet (ﷺ) or the Salaf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) اللهم ارزقني

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, du'a in prayer invalidates if it contains a request that could be fulfilled by a human being; otherwise it is permissible.

Excessive Action (Amal Kathir) and Invalidity of Prayer
Definition and examples of excessive action that invalidates prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of excessive action (al-amal al-kathir) and apply it to determine whether a given action invalidates prayer.

Core Idea
An action invalidates prayer if a neutral observer would think you are no longer praying.

The concept of excessive action (amal kathir) is crucial for preserving the sanctity and form of prayer. The Hanafi school provides detailed criteria to distinguish between movements that break prayer and those that are excusable. This lesson unpacks the primary definition, supporting examples, and various scholarly interpretations to help you apply the rule accurately.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary definition
The text states that excessive action is what makes a neutral observer (who does not know you are praying) doubt that you are in prayer. If the observer would be uncertain, the action is considered little and does not invalidate.
This is the most authoritative definition according to the passage.
2
Examine the examples
The passage gives concrete examples: three consecutive movements (fanning, scratching, throwing stones, plucking hairs) are excessive if done without interruption. If separated, they are not excessive.
Note the condition of consecutive action (al-wala').
3
Analyze the secondary definitions
Additional definitions include: what is normally done with both hands is excessive even with one hand; what is intended by the doer is excessive; and the most flexible view—delegated to the person's own judgment.
These views show the range of scholarly opinion within the Hanafi school.
4
Apply the definition to the puzzle
The passage includes a puzzle: a child drinking milk from his mother's breast three times invalidates her prayer. This illustrates that an action by another can affect the person's prayer if it causes a direct physical response (milk flow).
This helps test your understanding of the observer test in an unusual scenario.
5
Understand the authority
The author notes that many detailed rulings are derivations from later scholars, not directly from Abu Hanifa. The view delegating to personal judgment is considered closest to the Imam's opinion.
Be aware of the hierarchy of authority: founder Imam vs. later mashayikh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و يفسدها العمل الكثير لا القليل والفاصل بينهما أن الكثير هو الذي لا يشك الناظر لفاعله أنه ليس في الصلاة، وإن اشتبه فهو قليل. على الأصح وقيل في تفسيره: غير هذا كالحركات الثلاث المتواليات كثير ودونها قليل، ويكره رفع اليدين عند إرادة الركوع والرفع عندنا لا يفسد على الصحيح. قال ابن أمير حاج والمراد من الناظر من لا علم له بكونه في الصلاة، وإلا فمن المعلوم أنه لو شاهد شروع إنسان في الصلاة، ثم رأى منه ما ينافيها كأن تناول مشطاً، و سرح رأسه، أو لحيته مرات متواليات فإنه يفسد حتماً مع إنتفاء التيقن بأنه ليس في الصلاة فتنبه اهـ. فرع: يقع لغزاً، فيقال فيه أي بغيره شخص شرب ففسدت صلاة غيره بشربه، ولو لم يكن مقتدياً، ولا متيمماً، وجوابه صبي رضع ثدي امرأة ثلاثاً، ونزل لبنها فإنها تفسد صلاتها على الأصح أفاده الشرح. على الأصح كذا في التبيين، وهو قول العامة وهو المختار وهو الصواب كما في المضمرات. قوله ( كالحركات الثلاث المتواليات كثير) حتى لو روح على نفسه بمروحة ثلاث مرات أو حك موضعاً من جسده كذلك، أو ثلاثة أحجار أو رمی نتف ثلاث شعرات فإن كانت على الولاء فسدت صلاته وإن فصل لا تفسد وإن كثر، وفي الخلاصة وإن حك ثلاثاً في ركن واحد تفسد صلاته إذا رفع يده في كل مرة وإلا فلا تفسد لأنه حك واحد اهـ . وقيل : ما يقام باليدين عادة كثير وإن فعله بيد واحدة، وما يقام بيد واحدة قليل، وإن فعله بيدين، وقيل: إن الكثير ما يكون مقصوداً للفاعل والقليل بخلافه، وقيل: إنه مفوض إلى رأي المبتلي فإن استكثره فكثير، وإن استقله فقليل، وهذا أقرب الأقوال إلى رأي الإمام كما في التبيين . قال المصنف : وفروعهم في هذا الباب قد اختلفت ولم تتفرع كلها على قول واحد والظاهر أن أكثرها تفريعات من المشايخ لم تكن منقولة عن الإمام الأعظم.
And excessive action invalidates it, not little action; and the distinction between them is that excessive is what makes the observer not doubt that the doer is not in prayer; if it is ambiguous, it is little. This is the most correct view. And it is said in its interpretation otherwise: like three consecutive movements is excessive, less than that is little. And raising the hands when intending ruku' is disliked; it does not invalidate according to the correct view. Ibn Amir Haj said: by observer is meant one who does not know that he is in prayer; otherwise, it is known that if one sees a person start prayer, then sees him do something contradictory like taking a comb and combing his head or beard repeatedly, it invalidates definitively even though there is no certainty that he is not in prayer; so be aware. A secondary issue: a puzzle is presented: a person drinks, and another's prayer is invalidated by his drinking, even if not following (muqtadi) nor making tayammum; its answer: a child sucks a woman's breast three times and milk flows, then her prayer is invalidated according to the most correct view. The most correct view is thus in al-Tabyin, it is the general view, the chosen one, and correct as in al-Mudmarat. His statement: 'like three consecutive movements is excessive' even if he fans himself with a fan three times, or scratches a spot on his body similarly, or throws three stones, or plucks three hairs; if consecutive, his prayer invalidates; if separated, it does not invalidate even if many. In al-Khulasah: if he scratches three times in one pillar (rukun) it invalidates if he lifts his hand each time; otherwise it does not because it is one scratch. And it is said: what is usually done with both hands is excessive even if done with one hand; what is usually done with one hand is little even if done with both. And it is said: excessive is what is intended by the doer, little is otherwise. And it is said: it is delegated to the opinion of the one afflicted: if he considers it excessive, it is excessive; if little, little. This is closest to the view of the Imam as in al-Tabyin. The author said: their branches in this chapter differ, and not all branch from one statement; apparently most are derivations from the later scholars, not transmitted from the Great Imam.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage defines 'excessive action' (amal kathir) which invalidates prayer, as opposed to little action which does not. The primary definition given is that excessive action is what makes a neutral observer (who does not know the person is praying) doubt that the person is in prayer. If the observer would be uncertain, the action is considered little and does not invalidate. Then several secondary definitions are mentioned: three consecutive movements are excessive; less than three is little. Examples include fanning oneself three times, scratching three times with hand lifted each time, throwing three stones, plucking three hairs consecutively. If these are done with separation (e.g., in different rak'ahs or with pauses), they do not invalidate. Another definition: what is normally done with both hands is excessive even if done with one, and vice versa. Another: what is intended by the doer (i.e., a purposeful action) is considered excessive; unintentional movements are little. The most flexible view is that it is left to the judgment of the person: if he deems it excessive, it is; if little, it is. This last view is attributed to Abu Hanifa himself and is considered the closest to his opinion. The author (al-Tahtawi? or the original matn author?) notes that the many rulings found in later works are often derivations from later scholars (mashayikh) and not directly from the founding Imams. The passage also includes a puzzle about a child drinking milk invalidating his mother's prayer, which illustrates that an action by another can invalidate one's prayer if it directly affects the person (like milk flowing from the mother's breast).

Key Terms
العمل الكثير (al-amal al-kathir): Excessive action that distracts from the form of prayer and invalidates it if intentional or consecutive.
الناظر (al-nazir): An observer who does not know the person is in prayer; used as a test for excessiveness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Raising hands for ruku'
C) Fanning oneself three consecutive times

Key Takeaway
Excessive action (amal kathir) invalidates prayer; it is defined by the observer test and exemplified by three consecutive movements.

Turning Chest from Qibla Invalidates Prayer
Ruling on turning chest from qibla invalidating prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that turning the chest completely away from qibla invalidates prayer, and identify the two exceptions (overcoming wind and prayer of fear) and the condition that only full turning to east/west causes invalidation.

Core Idea
Turning the chest completely away from qibla invalidates prayer, except for sudden need or in fear prayer.

Facing the qibla is a condition of prayer. But what if you need to turn? This lesson explains that turning the chest entirely away—such that no part faces the qibla—invalidates the prayer, with two specific exceptions. We'll also clarify that slight deviations or partial turns do not harm, as long as some of the chest remains directed towards the qibla.

Guided Path
1
State the default ruling
Identify that turning the chest away from qibla invalidates (yu/sidu) the prayer. This is because it abandons the obligation of facing qibla (fard al-tawajjuh).
2
Identify the exceptions
Two cases do not invalidate: (1) when overcome by a sudden need for relief (hadath) such as passing wind, and (2) when one must turn to guard against the enemy in the prayer of fear (salat al-khawf).
The exception is for the act of turning itself; the prayer remains valid despite the turn.
3
Understand the 'chest' criterion
The earlier principle applies: one is considered facing qibla if at least a part of the chest is directed towards it. Turning only invalidates if the chest is turned completely to the west or to the east (i.e., no part remains facing the qibla).
Thus, turning slightly to the left or right while the chest still faces qibla does not invalidate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و يفسدها تحويل الصدر عن القبلة لتركه فرض التوجه إلا لسبق حدث أو لإصطفاف حراسة بازاء العد وفي صلاة الخوف . الظاهر أن حكم الصدر في الاستقبال الحكم السابق فيعد مستقبلاً باستقبال جزء منه ولا تفسد إلا بالتحويل إلى المغارب، أو إلى المشارق .
And turning the chest away from the qibla invalidates it because it abandons the obligation of facing the qibla, except for the gushing of wind (overcoming wind) or for aligning in guarding rows facing the enemy in the prayer of fear. The apparent rule is that the chest in facing qibla follows the earlier ruling: one is considered facing if a part faces, and it only invalidates if turned to the west or to the east.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This is a concise ruling: turning the chest completely away from the qibla invalidates the prayer because facing the qibla is an obligation (fard). However, there are exceptions: if one is overcome by wind (i.e., physical need causing involuntary movement) or if in the prayer of fear (salat al-khawf) where one must align to guard against the enemy, then turning is allowed and does not invalidate. The commentary clarifies that the earlier principle for facing qibla applies: if any part of the chest still faces the qibla, it is sufficient; complete invalidation only occurs when one turns fully to the west or east (i.e., opposite direction) such that no part of the chest is towards the qibla.

Key Terms
تحويل الصدر (tahwil al-sadr): Turning the chest so that no part faces the qibla.
فرض التوجه (fard al-tawajjuh): The obligation of orienting towards the qibla in prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) When done forgetfully
C) In the prayer of fear (salat al-khawf)
D) When one is alone

Key Takeaway
The prayer is invalidated only when the chest is turned completely away from the qibla, except for two excuses: being overcome by a necessary physical release or aligning in the prayer of fear.

Eating in Prayer Invalidates Even a Little
Ruling that eating anything from outside the mouth invalidates prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating during prayer and distinguish between external food and internal remnants.

Core Idea
Eating even a sesame seed from outside invalidates prayer; internal remnants do not.

The ruling on eating during prayer is strict: any external food, no matter how small, breaks the prayer because it is avoidable. However, swallowing food already in the mouth from before prayer does not affect validity. Let's examine the precise conditions and rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The act is eating (akl). The ruling: it invalidates the prayer (yufsid).
Eating includes drinking in Hanafi terminology.
2
Identify the condition
The eating must be 'from outside his mouth' (min kharij famihi). Even a tiny amount like a sesame seed suffices.
This condition excludes food already inside the mouth.
3
Understand the rationale
The reason given is 'it is possible to avoid it' (li-imkan al-ihrtraz). Avoidability is key: if the act is unavoidable, the ruling differs.
This explains why internal remnants are excused.
4
Distinguish from internal remnants
Swallowing leftover food from inside the mouth does not invalidate because it is not from outside and is not reasonably avoidable.
Contrast: a sesame seed from outside vs. a seed stuck in teeth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و يفسدها أكل شيء من خارج فمه ولو قل كسمسمة لإمكان الاحتراز عنه.
And eating anything from outside his mouth invalidates it, even if little, like a sesame seed, because it is possible to avoid it.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This is a straightforward ruling: eating any food that enters the mouth from outside (i.e., not something already inside the mouth before prayer) invalidates the prayer, regardless of the amount. Even a single sesame seed would invalidate it. The reason given is that it is possible to avoid eating while in prayer. This contrasts with the ruling on swallowing remnants of food already in the mouth (which does not invalidate) because that cannot be avoided without spitting. The text emphasizes that because one can choose not to eat during prayer, any consumption breaks the form of prayer.

Key Terms
أكل (akl): Ingesting any food or drink from outside the mouth into the body.
من خارج فمه (min kharij famihi): From outside his mouth; excludes swallowing leftover bits already in mouth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The prayer is invalidated
B) The prayer is valid and no compensation needed
C) It is makruh but valid
D) It requires sajdah al-sahw

Key Takeaway
Eating anything from outside the mouth invalidates prayer; internal remnants do not, because the former is avoidable.

Walking in Prayer: The Invalidating Distinction
Distinction between permissible and invalidating walking during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between permissible and invalidating walking in prayer and state the conditions under which walking invalidates.

Core Idea
Only consecutive excessive walking without excuse invalidates prayer; non-consecutive walking does not.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rule on walking (mashy) during prayer. The classical texts distinguish between interrupted and continuous walking, with different rulings. Understanding this distinction helps maintain the validity of salah while accommodating necessary movement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of walking
The passage describes two types: non-consecutive (mashyan ghayr mutadarrik) where the person walks a small amount, pauses for a rukun, then walks again; and consecutive (mashyan mutalahiq) where steps are continuous and cover a significant distance (e.g., two rows at once).
The key is whether the walking is interrupted by prayer actions or pauses.
2
Determine the ruling for each type
For non-consecutive excessive walking, the prayer is not invalidated unless one leaves the mosque or passes rows in an open area. For consecutive excessive walking without excuse, the prayer is invalidated regardless of qibla orientation.
'Excessive' here is defined as walking two rows or more continuously.
3
Note the exceptions
Leaving the mosque (if praying inside) or passing beyond the rows (if in desert) invalidates the prayer even if the walking is non-consecutive. This is because these actions are considered excessive in themselves.
The ruling is stricter when it involves leaving the designated prayer area.
4
Consider the role of excuse
If walking is for an excuse (e.g., moving to avoid danger, adjusting alignment for prayer-of-fear), it is permissible and does not invalidate, regardless of continuity.
The presence of an excuse changes the hukm from haram/invalidating to permissible.
5
Apply to a scenario
Take a concrete case: a person in the mosque walks one row, stops for a tasbih, walks another row, stops. This is non-consecutive; prayer valid. If he walks two rows without stopping, it is consecutive and invalid unless there is an excuse.
Practice applying the rule to ensure understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استثنى : إذا مشى في صلاته مشياً غير متدارك بأن مشي قدر صف، ووقف قدر ركن ثم مشى قدر صف آخر هكذا إلى أن مشى قدر صفوف كثيرة لا تفسد صلاته إلا إذا خرج من المسجد إن كان يصلي فيه أو تجاوز الصفوف في الصحراء فإن مشى متلاحقاً بأن مشى قدر صفين دفعة واحدة، أو خرج من المسجد أو تجاوز الصفوف في الصحراء فسدت صلاته اهـ وذكر المحقق ابن أمير حاج ما حاصله أن المشي لا يخلو إما أن يكون بلا عذر أو يكون بعذر فإن كان بلا عذر فإن كان كثيراً متوالياً يفسد صلاته سواء استدبر القبلة مع ذلك أو لا لأنه حينئذ عمل كثير ليس من أعمال الصلاة، ولم تقع الرخصة فيه وإن كان كثيراً غير متوال بل تفرق في ركعات أو تخلله مهلات فإن استدبر معه
Exception: if he walks in his prayer walking in a non-consecutive manner – he walks the length of one row, stops for the length of an integral, then walks another row, and so on until he walks many rows – his prayer is not invalidated unless he leaves the mosque if praying inside, or passes the rows in the desert. If he walks consecutively – walking the length of two rows at once, or leaves the mosque, or passes the rows in the desert – his prayer is invalidated. And the investigator Ibn Amir Hajj mentioned, summarized: walking is either without excuse or with excuse. If without excuse, then if it is excessive and consecutive, it invalidates his prayer whether he turns his back to the qibla or not, because then it is excessive action not from the actions of prayer, and there is no concession for it. And if it is excessive but non-consecutive, rather spread over rak'ahs or interspersed with pauses, then if he turns his back with it... (text cut off).
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage discusses walking (mashy) during prayer. It distinguishes between two types: interrupted walking (mashyan ghayr mutadarrik) where the person walks a small amount, pauses, walks again, etc., and consecutive walking (mashyan mutalahiq) where the steps are continuous. The general rule is: if one walks without an excuse, and the walking is excessive and consecutive, it invalidates the prayer regardless of whether the qibla orientation changes. However, if the walking is excessive but not consecutive (e.g., walk a row, stop, walk another row later), it does not invalidate unless one completely leaves the mosque (if praying inside) or passes beyond the rows if in an open area. The text also notes that if there is an excuse (such as moving to avoid danger or for prayer-of-fear), walking is permissible and does not invalidate. This ruling aligns with the larger principle that actions must be evaluated based on continuity and the perspective of an observer.

Key Terms
مشياً غير متدارك (mashyan ghayr mutadarrik): Walking that is not consecutive; interrupted by pauses or prayer actions.
مشياً متلاحقاً (mashyan mutalahiqan): Consecutive walking without pause, considered excessive action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which type of walking invalidates prayer if done without excuse?
A) Walking one row and stopping for a rukun, then walking another row
B) Walking two rows consecutively without pause
C) Walking one step to avoid an impurity
D) Walking while turning to the qibla

Key Takeaway
The validity of prayer during walking depends on whether the walking is consecutive and excessive; if non-consecutive, it remains valid unless leaving the mosque or passing rows.

Nullifiers of Prayer: Eating, Drinking, and Involuntary Speech
Refining nullifiers of prayer with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on actions that invalidate prayer (eating, drinking, clearing throat, groaning, and tashmit) and identify the conditions that excuse or invalidate, including the intra-Hanafi dispute on tashmit.

Core Idea
Prayer is invalidated by external speech unless it is from devotion; even groaning and tashmit invalidate if avoidable.

In this lesson, we examine a passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi that details various actions that invalidate prayer. Understanding these nullifiers is essential for preserving the validity of salah. The commentary refines the base text by introducing conditions such as the amount of food, the presence of an excuse, and the audibility of letters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
For each action mentioned (eating between teeth, drinking, clearing throat, sighing, groaning, tashmit), state the basic Hanafi ruling: does it invalidate prayer?
The text lists several actions with the phrase 'yufsidaha' (invalidates it).
2
Check the conditions that affect the ruling
Determine the qualifying conditions: for eating, the amount must be abundant (size of a chickpea) and avoidable; for clearing throat, it must be without excuse; for groaning/sighing, it must produce audible letters and be from worldly causes.
The commentary distinguishes based on 'imkan al-ihtiraz' (possibility of avoidance) and 'huruf masmu'ah' (audible letters).
3
Distinguish between speech by meaning and devotion
Groaning or sighing from pain or calamity is considered 'kalam bi ma'na' (speech by meaning) and invalidates; but from remembrance of Paradise or Hell does not invalidate due to indicating humility.
This distinction is based on intention and context.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on tashmit
Saying 'yarhamukallah' to a sneezer: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say it invalidates; Abu Yusuf says it does not. Identify the proof for each side and the mufta bihi.
The relied-upon position is that of the two (Sahibayn), so prayer is invalidated.
5
Apply the principle to subsidiary rulings
Consider additional cases: crying during prayer, saying 'bismillah' when stung, etc. Use the same criteria of avoidance and worldly intent.
The commentary includes furu' that test the boundaries of the rule.
