Book of Prayer: Guarding against it (and) invalidates it: eating what is between his teeth if it is abundant, and abundant is the amount of a chickpea, even if with little action because avoidance is possible, unlike a little with little action because it follows his saliva. And if with much action, it is invalidated by the action. (And) invalidates it: his drinking because it contradicts prayer. And if he raises his head to the sky and hail or rain falls into his throat and reaches his stomach, his prayer is invalidated. (And) invalidates it: clearing the throat without excuse because it contains letters, but if with an excuse, like preventing phlegm from recitation, it does not invalidate. (And) sighing like blowing dust and annoyance. (And) groaning: it is 'ah' with sukun on the ha, shortened like the weight of 'da'. (And) sighing: it is saying 'awh'. In it there are many languages, extended and not extended, with tashdid on the open waw, and sukun and kasra of the ha, and the raising of his crying. It is: that letters are produced audibly. And his statement: 'from pain in his body or calamity by loss of a beloved or wealth' is a restriction for groaning and what follows because it is speech by meaning. (No) it does not invalidate by its occurrence from remembrance of Paradise or Fire, unanimously, due to indicating humility. (And) invalidates it: saying yarhamukallah to a sneezer (with the mu'jama shin, more eloquent than the important) - the supplication for good directed to a sneezer by saying 'may Allah have mercy on you' according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) contrary to Abu Yusuf (in one narration? Actually 'khilafan li Abi Yusuf' means contrary to Abu Yusuf, so the two say it invalidates, Abu Yusuf says no). And answering a questioner about something heard...
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi explains various actions that invalidate prayer according to the Hanafi school. The commentary expands on the base text (Maraqi al-Falah) by detailing conditions and exceptions. The first ruling concerns eating what is between the teeth: if the amount is large (the size of a chickpea) and the person can avoid it, the prayer is invalidated, regardless of whether little action was involved. If the amount is small with little action (like swallowing saliva), it does not invalidate because it is treated as saliva. However, if small but requiring much action (like chewing), the prayer is invalidated due to the excessive movement. Similarly, drinking invalidates prayer because it contradicts the state of prayer, even if water enters unintentionally (like rain falling into the throat). Clearing the throat (tanahnuh) invalidates if done without excuse and produces audible letters; but if needed to clear phlegm for recitation, it is excused. Likewise, sighing (ta'fif) and groaning (anīn) and sighing (ta'awwuh) invalidate if they produce letters audibly and are avoidable. The commentary notes that if the groaning or sighing arises from pain or calamity, it is considered speech by meaning and thus invalidates, but if from remembrance of Paradise or Hell, it does not invalidate because it indicates humility and is not worldly speech. The next action is responding to a sneeze (tashmit): saying 'yarhamukallah' to a sneezer. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the two), this invalidates the prayer, contrary to Abu Yusuf who holds it does not. The relied-upon position is that it invalidates because it is a direct address to another person. Answering a questioner also invalidates. The commentary includes many subsidiary rulings (furu') about crying: if from awe of Allah and one says 'na'am' or 'bala' it does not invalidate; if from worldly matters, it does. Also, if stung by a scorpion and one says 'bismillah', it does not invalidate according to the fatwa. The passage emphasizes that the test is whether words are produced audibly and whether the action is intentional and avoidable.

Key Terms
الحمصة (al-ḥimmaṣa): The size of a chickpea – used to define the threshold for an abundant amount of food between the teeth that invalidates prayer.
التنحنح (al-tanaḥnuḥ): Clearing the throat to produce sounds; if done without excuse and produces letters, it invalidates prayer.
التشميت (al-tashmit): Responding to a sneezer by saying 'yarhamukallah'; according to the text, it invalidates prayer if done by the person praying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the threshold for a large amount of food between the teeth that invalidates prayer?
A) The size of a date
B) The size of a chickpea
C) The size of a fist
D) Any amount regardless of size

Key Takeaway
Prayer is invalidated by avoidable external speech that produces audible letters; however, speech from devotion or necessity is excused.

Answering with Quranic Verses in Prayer: Intention Invalidates
Intra-Hanafi dispute on invalidating prayer by responding with Quranic verses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why answering a question with a Quranic verse during prayer invalidates according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and evaluate Abu Yusuf's counter-argument.

Core Idea
Intention transforms Quranic recitation into speech — answering with a verse invalidates prayer unless it is mere recitation.

The prayer is a state of exclusive devotion to Allah. When a worshipper uses a Quranic verse to respond to someone, the words cease to be worship and become ordinary speech. This lesson explores the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether this invalidates prayer, focusing on the role of intention (niyya). We will examine the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf, and understand the underlying principle that distinguishes recitation as worship from recitation as conversation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is reciting a Quranic verse as a response to a question during prayer. State the general ruling: it invalidates according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the majority).
The act of answering is separate from mere recitation.
2
Locate the disagreement
Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that the words remain praise (thana') and their essential nature does not change by intention. Therefore the prayer remains valid.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not between madhahib.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that the verse becomes a response (jawab), which makes it speech (kalam) of people. The hadith prohibits all speech of people in prayer. Intention (qasd) transforms the recitation into a conversational tool, invalidating the prayer.
The principle: recitation is worship; response is speech.
4
Evaluate Abu Yusuf's counter-argument
Abu Yusuf claims that the words of the Qur'an are inherently praise and cannot be changed by the reciter's intention. The prayer remains valid because the utterance is still from the Qur'an.
He focuses on the literal nature of the words, not the speaker's intent.
5
Determine the tarjih
The majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is mufta bihi. The ruling is that answering with a Quranic verse invalidates prayer.
This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجواب مستفهم عن سبحانه أي: قال هل مع الله إله آخر فأجابه المصلي (بلا إله إلا الله) يفسد عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف هو يقول أنه ثناء لا يتغير بعزيمته وهما يقولان أنه صار جواباً فيكون متكلماً بالمنافي (وخبر سوء بالاسترجاع إنا لله وإنا إليه راجعون (وساز بالحمد لله و) جواب خبر عجب بلا إله إلا الله أو بسبحان الله و يفسدها (كل شيء) من القرآن (قصد به الجواب كيا خذ الكتاب لمن طلب كتاباً ونحوه، وقوله : آتنا غداءنا لمستفهم عن الإتيان بشيء، وتلك حدود الله فلا تقربوها نهياً لمن استأذن في الأخذ وهكذا، وإذا لم يرد به الجواب، بل أراد إعلام أنه في الصلاة لا تفسد بالاتفاق. قوله: (إنما قيد بالقرآن ليعلم الحكم في غيره بالأولى، فلو ذكر الشهادتين عند ذكر المؤذن لهما، أو سمع ذكر الله ، فقال : جل جلاله أو ذكر النبي ا ا فصلى عليه، أو قال عند ختم الإمام القراءة صدق الله العظيم أو صدق رسوله، أو سمع الشيطان فلعنه أو ناداه رجل بأن يجهر بالتكبير ففعل فسدت فإن قيل : روي أنه قال في جواب ابن مسعود حين استأذن عليه في الدخول، وهو في الصلاة : ادخلوها بسلام آمنين ولم تفسد الصلاة أجاب عنه بأنه محمول على أنه انتهى بالقراءة إلى هذا الموضع ولم يرد به الخطاب كما في شروح الهداية.
And the answer of a questioner about 'subhanahu' i.e., saying 'Is there another god with Allah?' and the praying person answers with 'La ilaha illallah' invalidates according to the two (Imam Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf. He (Abu Yusuf) says it is praise that does not change by his intention, but they (the two) say it has become an answer, so he is speaking contradictory (to prayer). And (responding to) evil news with 'inna lillahi wa inna ilayhi raji'un' and (responding to) good news with 'alhamdulillah,' and answering news of wonder with 'la ilaha illallah' or 'subhanallah,' and it invalidates (the prayer). Every verse of the Qur'an intended as an answer, like saying 'Khudh al-Kitab' (Take the Book) to someone asking for a book, and similar; and his saying 'Atina ghada'ana' (Give us our morning meal) to a questioner about bringing something; and 'Tilka hudud Allahi fala taqrabuha' (These are the limits of Allah, so do not approach them) as a prohibition for someone seeking permission to take; and so on. And if he did not intend it as an answer, but rather intended to inform that he is in prayer, it does not invalidate by consensus. His statement: 'It is qualified by the Qur'an to let it be known that the ruling for other than it is even more so. For if he mentioned the two testimonies when the mu'adhdhin mentioned them, or heard the mention of Allah and said 'Jalla jalaluhu,' or heard the mention of the Prophet and prayed upon him, or said at the end of the Imam's recitation 'Sadaqa Allahu al-'Azim' or 'Sadaqa Rasuluhu,' or heard the shaytan and cursed him, or a man called out to him to say the takbir loudly and he did so, it invalidates. For if it is said: It is narrated that he said in answer to Ibn Mas'ud when he asked permission to enter while he was in prayer: 'Udkhuluha bi-salamin aminin' (Enter it in peace, secure) and the prayer was not invalidated, the answer is that it is interpreted to mean that he had reached that verse in his recitation and did not intend to address him, as in the commentaries of al-Hidayah.'
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling on using Quranic verses to answer questions or respond to situations during prayer. The matn states that answering a questioner who says 'Subhanallah' (meaning 'Is there another god with Allah?') with 'La ilaha illallah' invalidates the prayer according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that it remains praise (dhikr). The majority consider it an answer, thus speech contrary to prayer. Other examples: responding to bad news with 'Inna lillah,' good news with 'Alhamdulillah,' or wonder with 'La ilaha illallah' or 'Subhanallah' all invalidate. The key principle is that any verse of the Qur'an recited with the intention of answering a question (like saying 'Khudh al-Kitab' to someone asking for a book) invalidates. However, if the recitation is without answering intent (e.g., merely informing the questioner that one is in prayer), it does not invalidate by consensus. The commentary extends this: other phrases like saying 'Jalla jalaluhu' upon hearing Allah's name, or sending salawat upon hearing the Prophet's name, or saying 'Sadaqa Allah' after Imam's recitation, all invalidate if done as responses. A potential counterexample (the Prophet replying to Ibn Mas'ud with a verse) is explained away by saying the Prophet was simply reciting that verse at that moment, not intending to answer.

Key Terms
جواب (jawab): A response or answer, which if intended with Quranic words, turns the recitation into speech (kalam) that invalidates prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The prayer is invalidated.
B) The prayer is valid because it is dhikr.
C) The prayer is makruh but valid.
D) The prayer is invalid only if said aloud.

Key Takeaway
Intention transforms Quranic recitation into speech in prayer; responding with a verse invalidates the prayer according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

When Praise Becomes Invalidating Speech in Prayer
Explains a dispute over whether saying 'la ilaha illallah' in response to a verse invalidates prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf regarding saying 'La ilaha illallah' as a response in prayer, and apply the hadith of Mu'awiyah ibn al-Hakam to distinguish permissible praise from invalidating human speech based on intent.

Core Idea
In prayer, what is normally praise becomes invalidating speech when uttered as a direct response to someone.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute over a specific situation: a worshipper hears a verse that poses a question and responds with 'La ilaha illallah'. The disagreement centers on whether the act invalidates prayer. Understanding this case sharpens your ability to apply the principle that intent determines whether words are permissible remembrance or prohibited human speech.

Guided Path
1
State the majority Hanafi position
Identify what Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold. They say the prayer is invalidated because the phrase becomes a response (jawab), making it speech of people (kalam al-nas).
This is the position of the two major imams of the school.
2
Identify the opposing view within the Hanafi school
Abu Yusuf disagrees. He argues that the phrase remains thana' (praise) and supplication, regardless of being a response, so it does not invalidate.
Abu Yusuf focuses on the intrinsic meaning of the words.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's proof
Abu Yusuf claims that even if the worshipper intends to answer, the words themselves are still praise and a request for forgiveness, which are permissible in prayer.
He sees the phrase as inherently a dhikr.
4
Examine the response of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
They counter with the hadith of Mu'awiyah ibn al-Hakam: the Prophet prohibited any speech of people in prayer. By responding, the worshipper turns the phrase into human speech, even if the words are normally good.
The hadith is explicit: 'This prayer of ours does not permit any of the speech of people.'
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the prayer is invalid and must be repeated; according to Abu Yusuf, it remains valid.
The mufta bihi generally follows the precautionary view of the majority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سبحانه أي :قال هل مع الله إله آخر فأجابه المصلي (بلا إله إلا الله) يفسد عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف هو يقول أنه ثناء لا يتغير بعزيمته وهما يقولان أنه صار جواباً فيكون متكلماً بالمنافي . وقال أبو يوسف لا تفسد لأنه دعاء بالمغفرة والرحمة . وجه قول الإمام حديث معاوية بن الحكم أن النبي الله قال لحين شمت العاطس أن صلاتنا هذه لا يصلح فيها شيء من كلام الناس وهو غير صالح في الصلاة
Glorified be He, meaning: He said 'Is there any other god with Allah?' and the praying person answered him (with 'There is no god but Allah'), it invalidates according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf. He (Abu Yusuf) says it is praise that does not change by his determination, and the two say it became a response, so he becomes one speaking what is contrary (to prayer). And Abu Yusuf said: It does not invalidate because it is a supplication for forgiveness and mercy. The proof of the Imam (Abu Hanifa) is the hadith of Mu'awiyah ibn al-Hakam that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to the one who responded to the sneezer: 'Indeed this prayer of ours does not permit any of the speech of people,' and it is not permissible in prayer.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a specific case: a praying person hears a reciter say the verse 'Is there any other god with Allah?' and he responds by saying 'La ilaha illallah'. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, this invalidates the prayer because the words are now serving as a response to a question, not as a mere recitation of praise. Abu Yusuf, however, argues that it does not invalidate because the words are inherently praise and supplication, and the intent does not change that. Abu Hanifa supports his view with the hadith of Mu'awiyah ibn al-Hakam where the Prophet forbade responding to a sneeze with 'Yarhamukallah' during prayer, indicating that any speech of people is incompatible with prayer. Thus, even a phrase that is normally good becomes invalid if used as a response to external speech.

Key Terms
ثناء (thana'): Praise or commendation of Allah, considered inherently permissible in prayer.
جواب (jawab): A direct answer to an external address, which is considered invalidating speech in prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because it is a supplication for the listener
D) Because the verse was from the Quran and false

Key Takeaway
Saying even words of praise in prayer invalidates if they are uttered as a direct response to someone, because the intent makes them human speech.

Responding to News in Prayer: What Invalidates
Ruling on saying istirja', tahmid, and tasbih in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the specific phrases that invalidate prayer when said as a response to external news and explain why the intent of response is the key factor.

Core Idea
Saying 'Inna lillah', 'Alhamdulillah', or 'Subhanallah' as a reaction to news invalidates your prayer.

In prayer, our speech must be purely for worship. When we hear good or bad news, our natural reaction is to say certain phrases. But if these phrases are said with the intent of responding to the news, they become human speech, not worship, and thus invalidate the prayer. This lesson clarifies which phrases cause invalidation and the underlying principle of intent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trigger events
The passage lists three types of external events: bad news, good news, and amazing/marvellous news. Identify each trigger.
Bad news: calamity. Good news: joy. Amazing news: wonderment.
2
List the corresponding responses
For each trigger, note the phrase that is customarily said: istirja' for bad news, Alhamdulillah for good news, and La ilaha illallah/Subhanallah for amazing news.
Istirja' = 'Inna lillahi wa inna ilayhi raji'un'.
3
State the ruling
When any of these phrases are said in prayer as a genuine response to the news, the prayer becomes invalid (fasid). The author says 'yufsidha' (it invalidates it).
Invalidation occurs regardless of whether the phrase itself is dhikr.
4
Understand the underlying principle
The ruling hinges on the intention (niyyah). If the phrase is said as a reaction to external events, it is considered speech (kalam al-nas), not worship. Only speech that is part of the prayer (Quran, dhikr, supplication) is allowed.
This principle is rooted in the prohibition of speaking in prayer.

Source Text

And (upon) bad news with saying 'Inna lillahi wa inna ilayhi raji'un' (and) good news with 'Alhamdulillah' and the answer to amazing news with 'La ilaha illallah' or 'Subhanallah' — it invalidates it (the prayer).
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage lays out specific cases where a praying person uses certain phrases in response to external events: upon hearing bad news, one says 'Inna lillah' (the istirja'); upon hearing good news, one says 'Alhamdulillah'; and upon hearing something amazing, one responds with 'La ilaha illallah' or 'Subhanallah'. If these are said with the intent of being a response to the news, the prayer is invalidated. The key is that these are common human responses, not acts of worship within the prayer. The subsequent commentary on the next page elaborates that if said without response intent (e.g., for teaching or seeking reward), the ruling varies. This is part of a broader principle that any speech directed at someone else or as a reaction to external events invalidates the prayer.

Key Terms
استرجاع (istirja'): Saying 'Inna lillahi wa inna ilayhi raji'un' upon calamity.
خبر عجب (khabar 'ajab): News that causes wonder or amazement.

Key Takeaway
In prayer, any phrase said as a reaction to external events (good, bad, or amazing) invalidates the prayer; always verify your intent before speaking.

General Rule: Quranic Verses as Answers
General rule: Quran recited as answer invalidates prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that reciting Quran as a response in prayer invalidates it, and distinguish when the recitation is intended as a response versus mere recitation.

Core Idea
Using Quranic verses to answer someone in prayer turns them into human speech—invalidating the prayer.

Prayer is a state of exclusive devotion to Allah. Any act that turns the words of the Quran into human communication breaks that devotion. This lesson clarifies a general Hanafi ruling: if a worshipper recites a Quranic verse with the specific intention of replying to someone outside the prayer, the prayer becomes invalid. However, if the same verse is recited without that intention, the prayer remains valid by consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act in question
Recognize that the act is reciting Quranic words during salah. The ruling depends on the intention behind the recitation.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
Scenario A: The worshipper recites a verse with the intention of answering someone (e.g., replying to a request). Scenario B: The worshipper recites the same verse without any such intention, perhaps to remind the questioner that he is in prayer.
3
Apply the ruling
Scenario A invalidates the prayer because the Quran is used as human speech. Scenario B does not invalidate by agreement among the scholars.
4
Recognize the examples given
The text mentions examples: 'Khudh al-kitab' (Take the book) for someone asking for a book; 'Atina ghada'ana' (Give us our morning meal) for someone inquiring about food; 'Tilka hududullahi fala taqrabuha' (These are the limits of Allah, do not approach them) for someone seeking permission to take something.
5
Note the exception
If the worshipper recites the verse merely to inform the speaker that he is in prayer (e.g., by reciting a verse that implies silence or devotion), then the prayer is not invalidated by consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويفسدها (كل شيء) من القرآن (قصد به الجواب کیا خذ الكتاب لمن طلب كتاباً ونحوه، وقوله : آتنا غداءنا لمستفهم عن الإتيان بشيء، وتلك حدود الله فلا تقربوها نهياً لمن استأذن في الأخذ وهكذا، وإذا لم يرد به الجواب، بل أراد إعلام أنه في الصلاة لا تفسد بالاتفاق
And it invalidates it (every) thing from the Quran that is intended as a response, like 'Take the book' for someone who requested a book and the like, and his saying 'Give us our morning meal' for someone inquiring about bringing something, and 'These are the limits of Allah, do not approach them' as a prohibition for someone who sought permission to take, and so on. And if he did not intend it as a response, but rather intended to inform that he is in prayer, it does not invalidate by agreement.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This is a general ruling that applies to any Quranic verse recited with the specific intention of answering a person outside the prayer. Examples: if someone asks for a book and the worshipper recites 'Khudh al-kitab' (Take the book) from the Quran; or if someone asks about bringing food and he recites 'Atina ghada'ana' (Give us our morning meal); or if someone seeks permission to take something and he recites 'Tilka hududullahi fala taqrabuha' (These are the limits of Allah, do not approach them). In all these cases, the Quranic verse is used as a direct answer, thus it becomes speech of people and invalidates the prayer. However, if the worshipper recites the verse not as an answer but merely to indicate that he is in prayer (e.g., by reciting a verse that implies he cannot talk), it does not invalidate by consensus. The key is the intention: if the recitation is meant to communicate with someone, it invalidates; if it is just recitation, it does not.

Key Terms
الجواب (al-jawab): Reciting Quran with the intention of replying to a human address.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Reciting any Quranic verse with the intention of answering someone outside the prayer invalidates the prayer; without that intention, it remains valid by consensus.

Muttayammim Seeing Water Before Sitting for Tashahhud: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on invalidation of mutayammim's prayer upon seeing water relative to tashahhud.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a mutayammim seeing water before and after sitting for tashahhud, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
When a mutayammim sees water after tashahhud, the prayer remains valid according to the Sahibayn but not according to Abu Hanifa.

Tayammum is a substitute for wudu; if water appears, the obligation to perform wudu returns. However, the timing of seeing water relative to the completion of prayer matters. This lesson examines the scenario where a mutayammim (or a follower behind such an imam) sees water before or after the final sitting (qadr al-tashahhud). The Hanafi school has an internal dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that the prayer is invalid until the salam, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) consider the prayer essentially complete after the tashahhud. We also explore a sub-case involving a follower who laughs loudly after seeing water.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
A person performing tayammum (or a follower behind a mutayammim imam) sees water that they can use. The key variable is whether this happens before or after they have sat for the duration of tashahhud (qadr al-tashahhud).
The sitting for tashahhud is considered the moment when all obligations (fara'id) of prayer are completed.
2
Identify the agreed and disputed timings
If water is seen before sitting for tashahhud, all Hanafi imams agree the prayer is invalid. If after, there is a dispute: Abu Hanifa says invalid; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say valid.
The dispute rests on whether prayer is complete after tashahhud or only after salam (which is wajib, not rukn).
3
Examine the sub-case of a follower laughing
The text discusses a follower behind a mutayammim imam who sees water and then laughs loudly (qahqaha). According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the invalidation is only in description (the prayer is void but the tahrimah remains), so the follower must still complete something? According to Muhammad and Zufar, the prayer is void from the start, so no nullification occurs.
This sub-case illustrates the practical implications of the dispute on the status of the prayer.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school for the main issue is that of the Sahibayn: if water is seen after sitting for tashahhud, the prayer is valid and need not be repeated.
This is based on the principle that the obligations of prayer are completed with the tashahhud, and only the exit (salam) remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

In summary, this issue is agreed upon regarding the invalidation of the prayer, but the Imam and Abu Yusuf invalidate it only in description, while Muhammad and Zufar invalidate it in description and origin. Therefore, they ruled no nullification by loud laughter in it because he was not in prayer originally. And there is no doubt that this ruling is not from the twelve rulings, so understand. His saying 'able to use it' – the pronoun in 'able' refers to the imam in both scenarios. His saying 'before his sitting the amount of tashahhud' – he only qualified it so that the invalidation is agreed upon, unlike when he sat the amount of tashahhud, where it does not invalidate according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), but it invalidates according to him (Abu Hanifa). For the two, these meanings, although they are invalidating like hadath and speech, their occurrence only comes after completion when nothing of the obligations remains.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario where a person performing tayammum (or a follower behind such an imam) sees water before sitting for tashahhud. The general rule: if the mutayammim sees water before the sitting of tashahhud, the prayer becomes invalid. The commentary introduces a sub-case: if a follower behind a mutayammim imam sees water and then laughs loudly (qahqaha), according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the prayer is invalidated but only in description (i.e., the follower must still complete the prayer? Actually, it means the prayer is void but the tahrimah remains? The text is complex). According to Muhammad and Zufar, the prayer is invalid from the start (asl), so the follower did not actually perform prayer, and thus there is no nullification by laughter. The key distinction is the timing: if the water is seen before sitting for tashahhud, all agree the prayer is invalid. If seen after sitting for tashahhud, only Abu Hanifa says it invalidates; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say it does not because the prayer was essentially complete, and only the exit remains. This is tied to the principle that the prayer becomes complete with the sitting of tashahhud; subsequent invalidators do not break it according to the two.

القعود قدر التشهد (al-qu'ud qadr al-tashahhud): Sitting for the duration required to recite the tashahhud, considered the final obligatory sitting.
التحريمة (al-tahrimah): The opening takbir that enters one into the state of ihram for prayer.

Key Takeaway
The timing of seeing water relative to tashahhud determines invalidation; after tashahhud, the prayer is complete according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Expiry of Masah Duration and Removal of Khuff During Prayer
Ruling on when expiry of masah or removal of khuff invalidates prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on when the expiry of the masah period or the removal of the khuff invalidates prayer, and identify the timing condition relative to sitting for tashahhud.

Core Idea
The expiration of masah or removal of khuff invalidates prayer only if it occurs before sitting for tashahhud.

This lesson addresses two specific invalidators of prayer for someone wearing khuff: the expiry of the permissible wiping period and the actual removal of the khuff. The key condition is the timing relative to the sitting for tashahhud. Understanding this nuance is essential for correctly assessing prayer validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts
First, recognize that the passage mentions two acts that affect prayer validity: the expiry of the masah period (for resident one day, for traveler three) and the removal of the khuff, even by a small action.
These are in addition to other nullifiers of prayer.
2
Note the timing condition
Both acts invalidate the prayer only if they occur before the worshipper sits for the amount of tashahhud. If they happen after that, the prayer remains valid according to the relied-upon view.
The sitting for tashahhud is a wajib in prayer.
3
Apply the ruling
Determine the exact moment: if the expiry or removal occurs before the sitting, the prayer is invalid and must be repeated. If after, the prayer is complete and valid.
The sequence matters: sitting first, then the event.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) كذلك (تمام مدة ماسح الخف) وتقدم بيانها (و) كذا نزعه) أي الخف، ولو بعمل يسير لوجوده قبل القعود قدر التشهد
And likewise (the expiry of the duration of the one wiping over khuff) and its explanation has preceded, and (the removal of it) i.e., the khuff, even if by a small action, because of its existence before the sitting of the amount of tashahhud.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage states two additional invalidators of prayer if they occur before sitting for tashahhud: (1) The expiry of the permissible period for wiping over khuff (which is one day and night for a resident, three days and nights for a traveler). (2) The actual removal of the khuff, even if done with a small action (like pulling it off). Both invalidate the prayer because they are akin to exposing the foot, which is a part that must be washed in wudu, and the prayer was based on the concession of wiping. The rule is tied to the timing: if these occur before the tashahhud, the prayer is invalid; if after, the prayer remains valid according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, but Abu Hanifa may differ as seen earlier. The commentary from the next page explains that for a resident it is one day and night, etc.

Key Terms
مدة المسح (muddah al-masah): The legally prescribed period for which wiping over khuff is permissible: 24 hours for a resident, 72 hours for a traveler.
خلع الخف (khal' al-khuff): Removal of the khuff, which nullifies the wiping concession and requires washing the feet.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The prayer is invalid
B) The prayer is valid
C) He must remove his khuff immediately
D) He must repeat wudu and continue

Key Takeaway
The expiry of masah or removal of khuff invalidates prayer only if it occurs before sitting for tashahhud; after that, the prayer remains valid.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
10 min
Three Cases of Changed Circumstances During Prayer
Ruling on three cases of changed circumstances during prayer before tashahhud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for each of the three cases (ummi learning a verse, naked person finding a covering, gesturing person regaining ability) and apply the principle that the prayer becomes invalid if the change occurs before tashahhud.

Core Idea
Changing your ability during prayer invalidates it if you were not qualified from the start.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi addresses three distinct scenarios where a person's situation changes during prayer: an illiterate person learns a verse, a naked person finds a covering, and one praying with gestures regains the ability to bow and prostrate. In each case, the key question is whether the prayer remains valid or must be restarted. The Hanafi ruling, as explained here, is that if the change occurs before sitting for tashahhud, the original prayer becomes invalid because the person's obligation has changed. Let's walk through each case step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first case: ummi learning a verse
The passage begins with 'وتعلم الأمي آية' – an illiterate person (unable to recite) learns a verse of the Quran during the prayer. Note the condition: he was not following a reciting imam. Determine the ruling: according to the Hanafi school, his prayer becomes invalid because his obligation to recite has changed from 'obligation to listen' (if following an imam) or 'no obligation' to 'obligation to recite'.
The text mentions a dispute: some say it does not invalidate. We'll examine that later.
2
Identify the second case: naked person finding a covering
The passage then discusses a person who was praying naked because no covering was available, but then finds a covering that meets the conditions (pure, permissible to use, owned or permitted by owner). The ruling: he must put it on and continue? Actually, the text says 'يلزمه الصلاة فيه' and implies the earlier prayer is invalid if found before tashahhud. The exception: if the covering is entirely najis or not permissible to use, then it is not considered.
The condition 'قدرة الموحي على الركوع والسجود' actually transitions to the third case. The text structure is a bit dense.
3
Identify the third case: gesturing person regaining ability
The phrase 'قدرة الموحي على الركوع والسجود' refers to a person who was praying with gestures (due to illness or injury) and then regains the ability to actually bow and prostrate. The ruling: 'فلا يبني' – he does not build upon his previous prayer; i.e., the previous prayer is invalid and he must start anew. The reason is that the ability to perform the actual pillars overrides the gesture-based concession.
The text says 'لقوة باقيها' – because of the strength of the remaining [limbs] – meaning the ability to use the remaining limbs overrides the excuse.
4
Apply the common principle: change before tashahhud
Although not explicitly stated in the Arabic clean, the commentary makes clear that these rulings apply when the change occurs before sitting for tashahhud. If it occurs after tashahhud, the validity may be preserved. This is a general Hanafi principle: circumstances that affect the validity of prayer are considered at the time of occurrence, and if they change before tashahhud, the original prayer is void.
This principle is rooted in the idea that the prayer is not considered complete until the final sitting.
5
Note the ikhtilaf (dispute) on the first case
The passage hints at an internal Hanafi dispute: some authorities (possibly from al-Zahiriyya) hold that learning a verse does NOT invalidate the prayer. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that it does invalidate. This illustrates that not all Hanafi positions are monolithic.
Check the key term 'al-ummi' for additional context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتعلم الأمي آية) ولم يكن مقتدياً بقارىء نسبة إلى أمة العرب الخالية عن العلم والكتابة كأنه كما وسواء تعلمها بالتلقي أو تذكرها ووجدان العاري ساتراً) يلزمه الصلاة فيه فخرج نجس الكل وما لم يبحه مالكه وقدرة الموحي على الركوع والسجود) لقوة باقيها فلا يبني
And the learning of an illiterate (ummi) a verse – and he was not following a reciter – attributed to the unlettered Arab nation devoid of knowledge and writing, as if he were... and whether he learned it by receiving or by recollecting, and the finding of a covering by the naked person – it is obligatory for him to pray in it, so excluded is entirely najis and that which its owner does not permit, and the ability of the one gesturing to bow and prostrate due to the strength of the remaining (limbs) – so he does not build upon (the previous prayer).
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage covers three distinct cases: (1) An illiterate person (ummi) who learns a verse during prayer. If he learns it before sitting for tashahhud, the prayer becomes invalid because he can now recite properly, and his previous prayer was deficient. The text mentions the condition that he is not following a reciting imam. There is a dispute: the common view says it invalidates, but a view in al-Zahiriyya says it does not. (2) A person who is naked finds a covering that he can wear (i.e., it is permissible for him to use, pure, and owned or permitted). In that case, he must wear it and his prayer is invalid if found before tashahhud. Excluded is clothing that is entirely najis or not permissible to use. (3) A person who was praying with gestures (due to inability to bow) regains the ability to bow and prostrate. According to this text, if this occurs before tashahhud, he does not build on his previous prayer; i.e., his previous prayer is invalid and he must start anew. The rationale is that the ability to perform the actual pillars overrides the gesture-based prayer. The commentary in later lines notes that if the ability occurs after tashahhud, the ruling may differ.

Key Terms
الأمي (al-ummi): A person who cannot recite Quran properly (illiterate in terms of reading/writing).
المومي (al-mūmī): A person who is unable to bow and prostrate and thus prays by gesturing with his head.
ما لم يبحه مالكه (mā lam yubaḥhu mālikuhu): That which its owner does not permit (i.e., usurped or stolen property).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an ummi (one who cannot recite) learns a verse during prayer before sitting for tashahhud and was not following an imam, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The prayer becomes more virtuous
B) The prayer becomes invalid
C) The prayer remains valid if he recites it silently
D) He should stop and restart after the prayer

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a change in a person's ability to fulfill prayer obligations (recitation, covering, physical movements) before tashahhud invalidates the prayer.
